<p><b>Alternative medicine</b> or <b>fringe medicine</b> are practices claimed to have the <a href="/wiki/Healing" title="Healing">healing</a> effects of <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a> but which are disproven, unproven, or impossible to prove, or are excessively harmful in relation to their effect; and where the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_consensus" title="Scientific consensus">scientific consensus</a> is that the therapy does not, or can not, work because the known <a href="/wiki/Scientific_law" title="Scientific law">laws of nature</a> are violated by its basic claims; or where it is considered so much worse than conventional treatment that it would be <a href="/wiki/Medical_ethics" title="Medical ethics">unethical</a> to offer as treatment. Alternative therapies or diagnoses are not part of <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a> or <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science-based</a> healthcare systems. Alternative medicine consists of a wide variety of practices, products, and therapies—ranging from those that are <a href="/wiki/Biologically_plausible" class="mw-redirect" title="Biologically plausible">biologically plausible</a> but not well tested, to those with known harmful and <a href="/wiki/Toxicity" title="Toxicity">toxic</a> effects. Contrary to popular belief, significant expense is paid to test alternative medicine, including over $2.5 billion spent by the United States government. Almost none show any effect beyond that of <a href="/wiki/Placebo" title="Placebo">false treatment</a>. Perceived effects of alternative medicine may be caused by <a href="/wiki/Placebo" title="Placebo">placebo</a>; decreased effect of functional treatment (and therefore potentially decreased <a href="/wiki/Side_effects" class="mw-redirect" title="Side effects">side effects</a>); and <a href="/wiki/Regression_toward_the_mean" title="Regression toward the mean">regression toward the mean</a> where improvement that would have occurred anyway is <a href="/wiki/Regression_fallacy" title="Regression fallacy">credited</a> to alternative therapies; or any combination of the above. Alternative treatments are neither the same as <a href="/wiki/Medical_research" title="Medical research">experimental medicine</a>, nor <a href="/wiki/Traditional_medicine" title="Traditional medicine">traditional medicine</a> — although the latter, when used today may be considered alternative.</p>
<p>Alternative medicine has grown in popularity and is used by a significant percentage of the population in many countries. While it has extensively rebranded itself: from quackery to complementary or integrative medicine—it promotes essentially the same practices. Newer proponents often suggest alternative medicine be used together with functional medical treatment, in a <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">belief</a> that it "complements" (improves the effect of, or mitigates the side effects of) the treatment. There is no evidence showing they do so, and significant <a href="/wiki/Drug_interaction" title="Drug interaction">drug interactions</a> caused by alternative therapies may instead negatively influence treatments, making them less effective, notably <a href="/wiki/Treatment_of_cancer" title="Treatment of cancer">cancer therapy</a>. Despite being illegal to market alternative therapies for cancer treatment in most of the developed world, many cancer patients use them.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Category:Alternative_medical_diagnostic_methods" title="Category:Alternative medical diagnostic methods">Alternative medical diagnoses</a> and treatments are not taught as part of science-based curricula in <a href="/wiki/Medical_schools" class="mw-redirect" title="Medical schools">medical schools</a>, and are not used in any practice where treatment is based on scientific knowledge or proven experience. Alternative therapies are often based on <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a>, tradition, <a href="/wiki/Superstition" title="Superstition">superstition</a>, belief in <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a> energies, <a href="/wiki/Pseudoscience" title="Pseudoscience">pseudoscience</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fallacy" title="Fallacy">errors in reasoning</a>, propaganda, fraud, or lies. Regulation and licensing of alternative medicine and <a href="/wiki/Health_care_provider" class="mw-redirect" title="Health care provider">health care providers</a> varies between and within countries.</p>
<p>Alternative medicine is criticized for being based on misleading statements, <a href="/wiki/Quackery" title="Quackery">quackery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pseudoscience" title="Pseudoscience">pseudoscience</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antiscience" title="Antiscience">antiscience</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fraud" title="Fraud">fraud</a>, or poor <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific methodology</a>. Promoting alternative medicine has been called dangerous and <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">unethical</a>. Testing alternative medicine that has no scientific basis has been called a waste of scarce research resources. Critics state "there is really no such thing as alternative medicine, just medicine that works and medicine that doesn't", and the problem with the idea of "alternative" treatments in this sense is that the "underlying logic is magical, childish or downright absurd". It has been strongly suggested that the very idea of any alternative treatment that works is <a href="/wiki/Paradox" title="Paradox">paradoxical</a>, as any treatment proven to work is by definition "medicine".</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Appeal">Appeal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Appeal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%22Miracle_Cure!%22_Health_Fraud_Scams_(8528312890).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/%22Miracle_Cure%21%22_Health_Fraud_Scams_%288528312890%29.jpg/220px-%22Miracle_Cure%21%22_Health_Fraud_Scams_%288528312890%29.jpg" width="220" height="285" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/%22Miracle_Cure%21%22_Health_Fraud_Scams_%288528312890%29.jpg/330px-%22Miracle_Cure%21%22_Health_Fraud_Scams_%288528312890%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/%22Miracle_Cure%21%22_Health_Fraud_Scams_%288528312890%29.jpg/440px-%22Miracle_Cure%21%22_Health_Fraud_Scams_%288528312890%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2550" data-file-height="3300" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%22Miracle_Cure!%22_Health_Fraud_Scams_(8528312890).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Alternative therapies often make bombastic claims, and frequently include anecdotes from healthy-looking individuals claiming successful treatment.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Practitioners of complementary medicine usually discuss and advise patients as to available alternative therapies. Patients often express interest in mind-body complementary therapies because they offer a non-drug approach to treating some health conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-Sobel2000_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sobel2000-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (November 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In addition to the social-cultural underpinnings of the popularity of alternative medicine, there are several psychological issues that are critical to its growth. One of the most critical is the placebo effect—a well-established observation in medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-van_Deventer2008_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-van_Deventer2008-2">[2]</a></sup> Related to it are similar psychological effects, such as the will to believe,<sup id="cite_ref-Beyerstein_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beyerstein-3">[3]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_bias" title="Cognitive bias">cognitive biases</a> that help maintain self-esteem and promote harmonious social functioning,<sup id="cite_ref-Beyerstein_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beyerstein-3">[3]</a></sup> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Post_hoc,_ergo_propter_hoc" class="mw-redirect" title="Post hoc, ergo propter hoc">post hoc, ergo propter hoc</a></i> fallacy.<sup id="cite_ref-Beyerstein_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beyerstein-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marketing">Marketing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Marketing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Edzard_ernst.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Edzard_ernst.jpg/220px-Edzard_ernst.jpg" width="220" height="186" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Edzard_ernst.jpg/330px-Edzard_ernst.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Edzard_ernst.jpg/440px-Edzard_ernst.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1972" data-file-height="1664" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Edzard_ernst.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Edzard_Ernst" title="Edzard Ernst">Edzard Ernst</a>, a leading authority on scientific study of alternative treatments and diagnoses, and the first university professor of <i>Complementary and Alternative Medicine</i>. Here in 2012, promoting his book <a href="/wiki/Trick_or_Treatment" class="mw-redirect" title="Trick or Treatment">Trick or Treatment</a> co-written with <a href="/wiki/Simon_Singh" title="Simon Singh">Simon Singh</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>CAM's popularity may be related to other factors that <a href="/wiki/Edzard_Ernst" title="Edzard Ernst">Edzard Ernst</a> mentioned in an interview in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Independent" title="The Independent">The Independent</a></i>:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Why is it so popular, then? Ernst blames the providers, customers and the doctors whose neglect, he says, has created the opening into which alternative therapists have stepped. "People are told lies. There are 40 million websites and 39.9 million tell lies, sometimes outrageous lies. They mislead cancer patients, who are encouraged not only to pay their last penny but to be treated with something that shortens their lives. "At the same time, people are gullible. It needs gullibility for the industry to succeed. It doesn't make me popular with the public, but it's the truth.<sup id="cite_ref-Con.3F_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Con.3F-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Paul_Offit" title="Paul Offit">Paul Offit</a> proposed that "alternative medicine becomes quackery" in four ways: by recommending against conventional therapies that are helpful, promoting potentially harmful therapies without adequate warning, draining patients' bank accounts, or by promoting "magical thinking."<sup id="cite_ref-Offit2013_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Offit2013-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="A_failure_of_mainstream_medicine">A failure of mainstream medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: A failure of mainstream medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lemon-906141.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Lemon-906141.jpg/220px-Lemon-906141.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Lemon-906141.jpg/330px-Lemon-906141.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Lemon-906141.jpg/440px-Lemon-906141.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4842" data-file-height="3632" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lemon-906141.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Friendly and colorful images of herbal treatments may look less threatening or dangerous when compared to conventional medicine. This is an intentional marketing strategy.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In a paper published in October 2010 entitled <i>The public's enthusiasm for complementary and alternative medicine amounts to a critique of mainstream medicine</i>, Ernst described these views in greater detail and concluded:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>[CAM] is popular. An analysis of the reasons why this is so points towards the therapeutic relationship as a key factor. Providers of CAM tend to build better therapeutic relationships than mainstream healthcare professionals. In turn, this implies that much of the popularity of CAM is a poignant criticism of the failure of mainstream healthcare. We should consider it seriously with a view of improving our service to patients.<sup id="cite_ref-Ernst2010_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ernst2010-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_factors">Social factors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Social factors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Authors have speculated on the socio-cultural and psychological reasons for the appeal of alternative medicines among the minority using them <i>in lieu</i> of conventional medicine. There are several socio-cultural reasons for the interest in these treatments centered on the low level of <a href="/wiki/Scientific_literacy" title="Scientific literacy">scientific literacy</a> among the public at large and a concomitant increase in <a href="/wiki/Antiscience" title="Antiscience">antiscientific</a> attitudes and <a href="/wiki/New_age" class="mw-redirect" title="New age">new age</a> <a href="/wiki/Mysticism" title="Mysticism">mysticism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Beyerstein_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beyerstein-3">[3]</a></sup> Related to this are vigorous <a href="/wiki/Marketing" title="Marketing">marketing</a><sup id="cite_ref-Weber_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weber-7">[7]</a></sup> of extravagant claims by the alternative medical community combined with inadequate media scrutiny and attacks on critics.<sup id="cite_ref-Beyerstein_3-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beyerstein-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Beyerstein2001_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beyerstein2001-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>There is also an increase in <a href="/wiki/Conspiracy_theory" title="Conspiracy theory">conspiracy theories</a> toward conventional medicine and pharmaceutical companies, mistrust of traditional authority figures, such as the physician, and a dislike of the current delivery methods of scientific biomedicine, all of which have led patients to seek out alternative medicine to treat a variety of ailments.<sup id="cite_ref-Beyerstein2001_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beyerstein2001-8">[8]</a></sup> Many patients lack access to contemporary medicine, due to a lack of private or public <a href="/wiki/Health_insurance" title="Health insurance">health insurance</a>, which leads them to seek out lower-cost alternative medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-Barnes2004_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes2004-9">[9]</a></sup> Medical doctors are also aggressively marketing alternative medicine to profit from this market.<sup id="cite_ref-Weber_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weber-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>Patients can be averse to the painful, unpleasant, and sometimes-dangerous <a href="/wiki/Adverse_effect" title="Adverse effect">side effects</a> of biomedical treatments. Treatments for severe diseases such as <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a> and <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a> infection have well-known, significant side-effects. Even low-risk medications such as <a href="/wiki/Antibiotics" title="Antibiotics">antibiotics</a> can have potential to cause life-threatening <a href="/wiki/Anaphylactic" class="mw-redirect" title="Anaphylactic">anaphylactic</a> reactions in a very few individuals. Many medications may cause minor but bothersome symptoms such as cough or upset stomach. In all of these cases, patients may be seeking out alternative treatments to avoid the adverse effects of conventional treatments.<sup id="cite_ref-Beyerstein_3-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beyerstein-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Beyerstein2001_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beyerstein2001-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions_and_terminology">Definitions and terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Definitions and terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_medicine">Alternative medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Alternative medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>It is loosely as a defined set of products, practices, and theories that are believed or perceived by their users to have the healing effects of <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-NSFquote_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSFquote-11">[n 1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IOMquote_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IOMquote-13">[n 2]</a></sup> but whose <a href="/wiki/Efficacy" title="Efficacy">effectiveness</a> has not been clearly established using <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific methods</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-NSFquote_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSFquote-11">[n 1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Angellquote_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Angellquote-15">[n 3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-16">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hines_Sampson_Coulter_Sagan_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hines_Sampson_Coulter_Sagan-17">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IGPIAMAYP_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IGPIAMAYP-18">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NP_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NP-19">[16]</a></sup> or whose theory and practice is not part of <a href="/wiki/Biomedicine" title="Biomedicine">biomedicine</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-IOMquote_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IOMquote-13">[n 2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Harrisonquote_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harrisonquote-21">[n 4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WebMD2014quote_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WebMD2014quote-23">[n 5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NCCIH2quote_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIH2quote-25">[n 6]</a></sup> or whose theories or practices are directly contradicted by <a href="/wiki/Scientific_evidence" title="Scientific evidence">scientific evidence</a> or <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">scientific principles</a> used in biomedicine.<sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-16">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hines_Sampson_Coulter_Sagan_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hines_Sampson_Coulter_Sagan-17">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AMCER_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AMCER-26">[20]</a></sup> "Biomedicine" or "medicine" is that part of <a href="/wiki/Medical_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Medical science">medical science</a> that applies principles of <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Molecular_biology" title="Molecular biology">molecular biology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biophysics" title="Biophysics">biophysics</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Natural_science" title="Natural science">natural sciences</a> to <a href="/wiki/Clinical_practice" class="mw-redirect" title="Clinical practice">clinical practice</a>, using scientific methods to establish the effectiveness of that practice. Unlike medicine,<sup id="cite_ref-Harrisonquote_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harrisonquote-21">[n 4]</a></sup> an alternative product or practice does not originate from using scientific methodology, but may instead be based on <a href="/wiki/Testimonial" title="Testimonial">testimonials</a>, <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a>, tradition, <a href="/wiki/Superstition" title="Superstition">superstition</a>, belief in <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a> energies, <a href="/wiki/Pseudoscience" title="Pseudoscience">pseudoscience</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fallacy" title="Fallacy">errors in reasoning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Propaganda" title="Propaganda">propaganda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fraud" title="Fraud">fraud</a>, or other unscientific sources.<sup id="cite_ref-Angellquote_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Angellquote-15">[n 3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NSF_2002_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSF_2002-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-16">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hines_Sampson_Coulter_Sagan_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hines_Sampson_Coulter_Sagan-17">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <i>General Guidelines for Methodologies on Research and Evaluation of Traditional Medicine</i>, published in 2000 by the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO), complementary and alternative medicine were defined as a broad set of health care practices that are not part of that country's own tradition and are not integrated into the dominant health care system.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO_Trad_Med_Defs_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_Trad_Med_Defs-27">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWHO2000_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWHO2000-28">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>The expression also refers to a diverse range of related and unrelated products, practices, and theories ranging from biologically plausible practices and products and practices with some evidence, to practices and theories that are directly contradicted by basic science or clear evidence, and products that have been conclusively proven to be ineffective or even toxic and harmful.<sup id="cite_ref-IOMquote_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IOMquote-13">[n 2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CAM_Sointu_Nissen_Eisenberg_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CAM_Sointu_Nissen_Eisenberg-29">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DaguptaHStabler2011_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DaguptaHStabler2011-30">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>The terms<i>-Alternative medicine</i>, <i>complementary medicine</i>, <i>integrative medicine,</i> <i>holistic medicine</i>, <i>natural medicine</i>, <i>unorthodox medicine</i>, <i>fringe medicine</i>, <i>unconventional medicine</i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/New_age" class="mw-redirect" title="New age">new age</a> medicine</i> are used interchangeably as having the same meaning and are almost synonymous in some contexts,<sup id="cite_ref-Shapiro_08_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shapiro_08-31">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bombardieri2000_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bombardieri2000-32">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Shuval2012_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shuval2012-33">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Freedman2011_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Freedman2011-34">[28]</a></sup> but may have different meanings in some rare cases.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Marcia.angell.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Marcia.angell.jpg/220px-Marcia.angell.jpg" width="220" height="300" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/06/Marcia.angell.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="307" data-file-height="418" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Marcia.angell.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Marcia_Angell" title="Marcia Angell">Marcia Angell</a>: "There cannot be two kinds of medicine — conventional and alternative".</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The meaning of the term "alternative" in the expression "alternative medicine", is not that it is an effective alternative to <a href="/wiki/Medical_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Medical science">medical science</a>, although some alternative medicine promoters may use the loose terminology to give the appearance of effectiveness.<sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_16-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-16">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kolata_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kolata-35">[29]</a></sup> Loose terminology may also be used to suggest meaning that a dichotomy exists when it does not, e.g., the use of the expressions "western medicine" and "eastern medicine" to suggest that the difference is a cultural difference between the Asiatic east and the European west, rather than that the difference is between <a href="/wiki/Evidence-based_medicine" title="Evidence-based medicine">evidence-based medicine</a> and treatments that don't work.<sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_16-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-16">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Complementary.2Fintegrative_medicine">Complementary/integrative medicine <span id="Complementary_medicine"></span><span id="Integrative_medicine"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Complementary/integrative medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><b>Complementary medicine</b> (<b>CM</b>) or <b>integrative medicine</b> (<b>IM</b>) is when alternative medicine is used together with functional medical treatment, in a <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">belief</a> that it improves the effect of treatments.<sup id="cite_ref-FinalReport_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FinalReport-37">[n 7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NSF_2002_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSF_2002-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ernst_1995_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ernst_1995-38">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Joyce_1994_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Joyce_1994-39">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BMJ_May_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BMJ_May-40">[33]</a></sup> However, significant <a href="/wiki/Drug_interaction" title="Drug interaction">drug interactions</a> caused by alternative therapies may instead negatively influence treatment, making treatments less effective, notably <a href="/wiki/Treatment_of_cancer" title="Treatment of cancer">cancer therapy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-41">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Arye13_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arye13-42">[35]</a></sup> Both terms refer to use of alternative medical treatments alongside conventional medicine,<sup id="cite_ref-NCI_PDQ_CAM_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCI_PDQ_CAM-43">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Borkan2012_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borkan2012-44">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-whatiscam_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whatiscam-45">[38]</a></sup> an example of which is use of <a href="/wiki/Acupuncture" title="Acupuncture">acupuncture</a> (sticking needles in the body to influence the flow of a supernatural energy), along with using science-based medicine, in the belief that the acupuncture increases the effectiveness or "complements" the science-based medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-whatiscam_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whatiscam-45">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Allopathic_medicine">Allopathic medicine <span id="Allopathic_medicine"></span><span id="Allopathy"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Allopathic medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><b>Allopathic medicine</b> or <b>allopathy</b> is an expression commonly used by <a href="/wiki/Homeopathy" title="Homeopathy">homeopaths</a> and proponents of other forms of alternative medicine to refer to <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">mainstream medicine</a>. Specifically it refers to the use of pharmacologically active <a href="/wiki/Active_ingredient" title="Active ingredient">agents</a> or <a href="/wiki/Surgery" title="Surgery">physical interventions</a> to treat or suppress <a href="/wiki/Symptoms" class="mw-redirect" title="Symptoms">symptoms</a> or <a href="/wiki/Pathophysiology" title="Pathophysiology">pathophysiologic</a> processes of diseases or conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-FREEDICT_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FREEDICT-46">[39]</a></sup> The expression was coined in 1810 by the creator of <a href="/wiki/Homeopathy" title="Homeopathy">homeopathy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Hahnemann" title="Samuel Hahnemann">Samuel Hahnemann</a> (1755–1843).<sup id="cite_ref-Whorton2003_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whorton2003-47">[40]</a></sup> In such circles, the expression "allopathic medicine" is still used to refer to "the broad category of medical practice that is sometimes called Western medicine, <a href="/wiki/Biomedicine" title="Biomedicine">biomedicine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evidence-based_medicine" title="Evidence-based medicine">evidence-based medicine</a>, or modern medicine" (see the article on <a href="/wiki/Scientific_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Scientific medicine">scientific medicine</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-WHO_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO-48">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Use of the term remains common among homeopaths and has spread to other alternative medicine practices. The meaning implied by the label has never been accepted by conventional medicine and is considered pejorative.<sup id="cite_ref-Atwood2004_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Atwood2004-49">[42]</a></sup> More recently, some sources have used the term "allopathic", particularly American sources wishing to distinguish between <a href="/wiki/Comparison_of_MD_and_DO_in_the_United_States" title="Comparison of MD and DO in the United States">Doctors of Medicine (MD) and Doctors of Osteopathic Medicine (DO) in the United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Whorton2003_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whorton2003-47">[40]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NRMP_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NRMP-50">[43]</a></sup> William Jarvis, an expert on alternative medicine and public health,<sup id="cite_ref-Closer_to_Truth_Participants.7C_PBS_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Closer_to_Truth_Participants.7C_PBS-51">[44]</a></sup> states that "although many modern therapies can be construed to conform to an allopathic rationale (e.g., using a laxative to relieve constipation), standard medicine has never paid allegiance to an allopathic principle" and that the label "allopath" was from the start "considered highly derisive by regular medicine".<sup id="cite_ref-Jarvis_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jarvis-52">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many conventional medical treatments clearly do not fit the nominal definition of allopathy, as they seek to prevent illness, or remove it's cause.<sup id="cite_ref-Berkenwald1998_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berkenwald1998-53">[46]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Federspil2003_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Federspil2003-54">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="CAM">CAM</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: CAM">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>CAM is an abbreviation of complementary and alternative medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-ernstinterview_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ernstinterview-55">[48]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CassilethDeng2004_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CassilethDeng2004-56">[49]</a></sup> It has also been called sCAM or SCAM with the addition of "so-called" or "supplements".<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[51]</a></sup> The words balance and holism are often used, claiming to take into account a "whole" person, in contrast to the supposed reductionism of medicine. Due to its many names the field has been criticized for intense <a href="/wiki/Rebranding" title="Rebranding">rebranding</a> of what are essentially the same practices: as soon as one name is declared synonymous with <a href="/wiki/Quackery" title="Quackery">quackery</a>, a new name is chosen.<sup id="cite_ref-Shapiro_08_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shapiro_08-31">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditional_medicine">Traditional medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Traditional medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Traditional_medicine" title="Traditional medicine">Traditional medicine</a></div>
<p><b>Traditional medicine</b> refers to the pre-scientific practices of a certain culture, contrary to what is typically practiced in other cultures where medical science dominates.</p>
<p>"<a href="/wiki/Eastern_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern medicine">Eastern medicine</a>" typically refers to the traditional medicines of Asia where conventional bio-medicine penetrated much later.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Problems_with_definition">Problems with definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Problems with definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Prominent members of the science<sup id="cite_ref-Offit2013_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Offit2013-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dawkins2003a_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dawkins2003a-59">[52]</a></sup> and biomedical science community<sup id="cite_ref-Angell1998_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Angell1998-14">[12]</a></sup> say that it is not meaningful to define an alternative medicine that is separate from a conventional medicine, that the expressions "conventional medicine", "alternative medicine", "complementary medicine", "integrative medicine", and "holistic medicine" do not refer to any medicine at all.<sup id="cite_ref-Offit2013_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Offit2013-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Angell1998_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Angell1998-14">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dawkins2003a_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dawkins2003a-59">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fontanarosa1998_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fontanarosa1998-60">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>Others in both the biomedical and CAM communities say that CAM <i>cannot</i> be precisely defined because of the diversity of theories and practices it includes, and because the boundaries between CAM and biomedicine overlap, are porous, and change.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarrison.27s_Principles_of_Internal_Medicine20151chpt._14-E_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarrison.27s_Principles_of_Internal_Medicine20151chpt._14-E-20">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report200514.E2.80.9320_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report200514.E2.80.9320-61">[54]</a></sup> The expression "complementary and alternative medicine" (CAM) resists easy definition because the health systems and practices it refers to are diffuse, and its boundaries poorly defined.<sup id="cite_ref-CAM_Sointu_Nissen_Eisenberg_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CAM_Sointu_Nissen_Eisenberg-29">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERuggie2004.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DViDwi7s2VvMC.26pg.3DPA20_20.5D_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERuggie2004.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DViDwi7s2VvMC.26pg.3DPA20_20.5D-62">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[n 8]</a></sup> Healthcare practices categorized as alternative may differ in their historical origin, theoretical basis, <a href="/wiki/Medical_diagnosis" title="Medical diagnosis">diagnostic technique</a>, <a href="/wiki/Therapy" title="Therapy">therapeutic practice</a> and in their relationship to the medical mainstream.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESointu2012.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DXC2UUP36bjAC.26pg.3DPA13_13.5D_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESointu2012.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DXC2UUP36bjAC.26pg.3DPA13_13.5D-65">[57]</a></sup> Some alternative therapies, including <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">traditional Chinese medicine (TCM)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ayurveda" title="Ayurveda">Ayurveda</a>, have antique origins in East or South Asia and are entirely alternative medical systems;<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[58]</a></sup> others, such as homeopathy and chiropractic, have origins in Europe or the United States and emerged in the eighteenth and nineteenth centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGevitz1997.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DZIqk31BXpksC.26pg.3DPA603_603.E2.80.9333.5D_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGevitz1997.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DZIqk31BXpksC.26pg.3DPA603_603.E2.80.9333.5D-67">[59]</a></sup> Some, such as <a href="/wiki/Osteopathy" title="Osteopathy">osteopathy</a> and chiropractic, employ manipulative physical methods of treatment; others, such as <a href="/wiki/Meditation" title="Meditation">meditation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prayer" title="Prayer">prayer</a>, are based on <a href="/wiki/Mind-body_intervention" class="mw-redirect" title="Mind-body intervention">mind-body interventions</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report2005.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fbooks.nap.edu.2Fopenbook.php.3Frecord_id.3D11182.26page.3D18_18.5D_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report2005.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fbooks.nap.edu.2Fopenbook.php.3Frecord_id.3D11182.26page.3D18_18.5D-68">[60]</a></sup> Treatments considered alternative in one location may be considered conventional in another.<sup id="cite_ref-Nissen2013_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nissen2013-69">[61]</a></sup> Thus, chiropractic is not considered alternative in Denmark and likewise <a href="/wiki/Osteopathic_medicine_in_the_United_States" title="Osteopathic medicine in the United States">osteopathic medicine</a> is no longer thought of as an alternative therapy in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-Nissen2013_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nissen2013-69">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>Critics say the expression is deceptive because it implies there is an effective alternative to science-based medicine, and that <i>complementary</i> is deceptive because it implies that the treatment increases the effectiveness of (complements) science-based medicine, while alternative medicines that have been tested nearly always have no measurable positive effect compared to a <a href="/wiki/Placebo" title="Placebo">placebo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_16-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-16">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Skep_Dic_comp_med_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Skep_Dic_comp_med-70">[62]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Novella2010_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Novella2010-71">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gorski2010_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gorski2010-72">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Different_types_of_definitions">Different types of definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Different types of definitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>One common feature of all definitions of alternative medicine is its designation as "other than" conventional medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report2005.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fbooks.nap.edu.2Fopenbook.php.3Frecord_id.3D11182.26page.3D14_14.5D_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report2005.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fbooks.nap.edu.2Fopenbook.php.3Frecord_id.3D11182.26page.3D14_14.5D-73">[65]</a></sup> For example, the widely referenced<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report2005.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fbooks.nap.edu.2Fopenbook.php.3Frecord_id.3D11182.26page.3D19_19.5D_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report2005.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fbooks.nap.edu.2Fopenbook.php.3Frecord_id.3D11182.26page.3D19_19.5D-74">[66]</a></sup> descriptive definition of complementary and alternative medicine devised by the <a href="/wiki/National_Center_for_Complementary_and_Alternative_Medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="National Center for Complementary and Alternative Medicine">US National Center for Complementary and Integrative Health (NCCIH)</a> of the <a href="/wiki/National_Institutes_of_Health" title="National Institutes of Health">National Institutes of Health (NIH)</a>, states that it is "a group of diverse medical and health care systems, practices, and products that are not generally considered part of conventional medicine".<sup id="cite_ref-NCCIH1_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIH1-75">[67]</a></sup> For conventional medical practitioners, it does not necessarily follow that either it or its practitioners would no longer be considered alternative.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[n 9]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some definitions seek to specify alternative medicine in terms of its social and political marginality to mainstream healthcare.<sup id="cite_ref-Saks1992_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Saks1992-79">[70]</a></sup> This can refer to the lack of support that alternative therapies receive from the medical establishment and related bodies regarding access to <a href="/wiki/Medical_research#Funding" title="Medical research">research funding</a>, sympathetic coverage in the <a href="/wiki/Medical_literature" title="Medical literature">medical press</a>, or inclusion in the standard <a href="/wiki/Medical_education" title="Medical education">medical curriculum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Saks1992_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Saks1992-79">[70]</a></sup> In 1993, the <a href="/wiki/British_Medical_Association" title="British Medical Association">British Medical Association (BMA)</a>, one among many professional organizations who have attempted to define alternative medicine, stated that it<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[n 10]</a></sup> referred to "...those forms of treatment which are not widely used by the conventional healthcare professions, and the skills of which are not taught as part of the undergraduate curriculum of conventional medical and paramedical healthcare courses".<sup id="cite_ref-BMA_1993_1st_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BMA_1993_1st-80">[71]</a></sup> In a US context, an influential definition coined in 1993 by the Harvard-based physician,<sup id="cite_ref-Artus2007_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Artus2007-82">[72]</a></sup> David M. Eisenberg,<sup id="cite_ref-Eisenberg_Bio_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eisenberg_Bio-83">[73]</a></sup> characterized alternative medicine "as interventions neither taught widely in medical schools nor generally available in US hospitals".<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[74]</a></sup> These descriptive definitions are inadequate in the present-day when some conventional doctors offer alternative medical treatments and CAM introductory courses or modules can be offered as part of standard undergraduate medical training;<sup id="cite_ref-HOL_Kopelman_Wieland_Astin_Pelletier_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HOL_Kopelman_Wieland_Astin_Pelletier-85">[75]</a></sup> alternative medicine is taught in more than 50 per cent of <a href="/wiki/Medical_school_in_the_United_States" title="Medical school in the United States">US medical schools</a> and increasingly <a href="/wiki/Health_insurance_in_the_United_States" title="Health insurance in the United States">US health insurers</a> are willing to provide reimbursement for CAM therapies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report2005.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fbooks.nap.edu.2Fopenbook.php.3Frecord_id.3D11182.26page.3D17_17.5D.2C_.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fbooks.nap.edu.2Fopenbook.php.3Frecord_id.3D11182.26page.3D196_196.E2.80.93252.5D_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report2005.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fbooks.nap.edu.2Fopenbook.php.3Frecord_id.3D11182.26page.3D17_17.5D.2C_.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fbooks.nap.edu.2Fopenbook.php.3Frecord_id.3D11182.26page.3D196_196.E2.80.93252.5D-86">[76]</a></sup> In 1999, 7.7% of US hospitals reported using some form of CAM therapy; this proportion had risen to 37.7% by 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-Salomonsen_AHA_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Salomonsen_AHA-87">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>An expert panel at a conference hosted in 1995 by the US Office for Alternative Medicine (OAM),<sup id="cite_ref-Wieland2011_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wieland2011-88">[78]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[n 11]</a></sup> devised a <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_definition" title="Theoretical definition">theoretical definition</a><sup id="cite_ref-Wieland2011_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wieland2011-88">[78]</a></sup> of alternative medicine as "a broad domain of healing resources&#160;... other than those intrinsic to the politically dominant health system of a particular society or culture in a given historical period".<sup id="cite_ref-CAM_RMC1997_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CAM_RMC1997-91">[80]</a></sup> This definition has been widely adopted by CAM researchers,<sup id="cite_ref-Wieland2011_88-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wieland2011-88">[78]</a></sup> cited by official government bodies such as the UK Department of Health,<sup id="cite_ref-UKDoH_CAM_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UKDoH_CAM-92">[81]</a></sup> attributed as the definition used by the <a href="/wiki/Cochrane_Collaboration" class="mw-redirect" title="Cochrane Collaboration">Cochrane Collaboration</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-ZollChanCoulRobeAdamArtu_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ZollChanCoulRobeAdamArtu-93">[82]</a></sup> and, with some modification,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (June 2015)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata">– <a href="/wiki/Talk:Alternative_medicine#Dubious" title="Talk:Alternative medicine">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup> was preferred in the 2005 consensus report of the <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_Medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Institute of Medicine">US Institute of Medicine</a>, <i>Complementary and Alternative Medicine in the United States</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-IOMquote_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IOMquote-13">[n 2]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 1995 OAM conference definition, an expansion of Eisenberg's 1993 formulation, is silent regarding questions of the medical effectiveness of alternative therapies.<sup id="cite_ref-Hufford2004_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hufford2004-94">[83]</a></sup> Its proponents hold that it thus avoids <a href="/wiki/Relativism" title="Relativism">relativism</a> about differing forms of medical knowledge and, while it is an essentially political definition, this should not imply that the dominance of mainstream <a href="/wiki/Biomedicine" title="Biomedicine">biomedicine</a> is solely due to political forces.<sup id="cite_ref-Hufford2004_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hufford2004-94">[83]</a></sup> According to this definition, alternative and <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">mainstream medicine</a> can only be differentiated with reference to what is "intrinsic to the politically dominant health system of a particular society of culture".<sup id="cite_ref-Kopelman2004_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kopelman2004-95">[84]</a></sup> However, there is neither a reliable method to distinguish between cultures and <a href="/wiki/Subculture" title="Subculture">subcultures</a>, nor to attribute them as dominant or subordinate, nor any accepted criteria to determine the dominance of a cultural entity.<sup id="cite_ref-Kopelman2004_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kopelman2004-95">[84]</a></sup> If the culture of a politically dominant healthcare system is held to be equivalent to the perspectives of those charged with the medical management of leading healthcare institutions and programs, the definition fails to recognize the potential for division either within such an elite or between a healthcare elite and the wider population.<sup id="cite_ref-Kopelman2004_95-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kopelman2004-95">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Normative" title="Normative">Normative</a> definitions distinguish alternative medicine from the biomedical mainstream in its provision of therapies that are unproven, unvalidated, or ineffective and support of theories with no recognized scientific basis.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report2005.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fbooks.nap.edu.2Fopenbook.php.3Frecord_id.3D11182.26page.3D17_17.5D_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report2005.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fbooks.nap.edu.2Fopenbook.php.3Frecord_id.3D11182.26page.3D17_17.5D-96">[85]</a></sup> These definitions characterize practices as constituting alternative medicine when, used independently or in place of <a href="/wiki/Evidence-based_medicine" title="Evidence-based medicine">evidence-based medicine</a>, they are put forward as having the healing effects of medicine, but are not based on <a href="/wiki/Evidence" title="Evidence">evidence</a> gathered with the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NSF_2002_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSF_2002-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Angell1998_14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Angell1998-14">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NCI_PDQ_CAM_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCI_PDQ_CAM-43">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Borkan2012_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borkan2012-44">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NCCIH1_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIH1-75">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kong2005_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kong2005-97">[86]</a></sup> Exemplifying this perspective, a 1998 editorial co-authored by <a href="/wiki/Marcia_Angell" title="Marcia Angell">Marcia Angell</a>, a former editor of the <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_England_Journal_of_Medicine" title="The New England Journal of Medicine">New England Journal of Medicine</a></i>, argued that:</p>
<dl>
<dd>"It is time for the scientific community to stop giving alternative medicine a free ride. There cannot be two kinds of medicine – conventional and alternative. There is only medicine that has been adequately tested and medicine that has not, medicine that works and medicine that may or may not work. Once a treatment has been tested rigorously, it no longer matters whether it was considered alternative at the outset. If it is found to be reasonably safe and effective, it will be accepted. But assertions, speculation, and testimonials do not substitute for evidence. Alternative treatments should be subjected to scientific testing no less rigorous than that required for conventional treatments."<sup id="cite_ref-Angell1998_14-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Angell1998-14">[12]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>This line of division has been subject to criticism, however, as not all forms of standard medical practice have adequately demonstrated evidence of benefit,<sup id="cite_ref-Harrisonquote_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harrisonquote-21">[n 4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ezzo2001_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ezzo2001-98">[87]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report200517.E2.80.9318_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report200517.E2.80.9318-99">[88]</a></sup> and it is also unlikely in most instances that conventional therapies, if proven to be ineffective, would ever be classified as CAM.<sup id="cite_ref-Wieland2011_88-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wieland2011-88">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>Similarly, the public information website maintained by the <a href="/wiki/National_Health_and_Medical_Research_Council" title="National Health and Medical Research Council">National Health and Medical Research Council</a> (NHMRC) of the Commonwealth of Australia uses the acronym "CAM" for a wide range of health care practices, therapies, procedures and devices not within the domain of conventional medicine. In the Australian context this is stated to include acupuncture; aromatherapy; chiropractic; homeopathy; massage; meditation and relaxation therapies; naturopathy; osteopathy; reflexology, traditional Chinese medicine; and the use of vitamin supplements.<sup id="cite_ref-NHMRC_CAM_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NHMRC_CAM-100">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/National_Board_of_Health_(Denmark)" title="National Board of Health (Denmark)">Danish National Board of Health's</a> "Council for Alternative Medicine" (Sundhedsstyrelsens Råd for Alternativ Behandling (SRAB)), an independent institution under the National Board of Health (Danish: <i>Sundhedsstyrelsen</i>), uses the term "alternative medicine" for:</p>
<ul>
<li>Treatments performed by therapists that are not authorized healthcare professionals.</li>
<li>Treatments performed by authorized healthcare professionals, but those based on methods otherwise used mainly outside the healthcare system. People without a healthcare authorisation are [also] allowed to perform the treatments.<sup id="cite_ref-VIFAB_definition_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VIFAB_definition-101">[90]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Proponents of an <a href="/wiki/Evidence-based_medicine" title="Evidence-based medicine">evidence-base for medicine</a><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[n 12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nuffield_Trust_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nuffield_Trust-104">[92]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Belsey2009_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Belsey2009-105">[93]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nuffield2_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nuffield2-106">[94]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bardsley2011_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bardsley2011-107">[95]</a></sup> such as the Cochrane Collaboration (founded in 1993 and from 2011 providing input for WHO resolutions) take a position that <i>all</i> systematic reviews of treatments, whether "mainstream" or "alternative", ought to be held to the current standards of scientific method.<sup id="cite_ref-UM_CAM_Field_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UM_CAM_Field-108">[96]</a></sup> In a study titled <i>Development and classification of an operational definition of complementary and alternative medicine for the Cochrane Collaboration</i> (2011) it was proposed that indicators that a therapy is accepted include government licensing of practitioners, coverage by <a href="/wiki/Health_insurance" title="Health insurance">health insurance</a>, statements of approval by government agencies, and recommendation as part of a practice guideline; and that if something is currently a standard, accepted therapy, then it is not likely to be widely considered as CAM.<sup id="cite_ref-Wieland2011_88-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wieland2011-88">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="NCCIH_classification">NCCIH classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: NCCIH classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A United States government agency, the National Center on Complementary and Integrative Health (<a href="/wiki/NCCIH" class="mw-redirect" title="NCCIH">NCCIH</a>), created its own classification system for branches of complementary and alternative medicine that divides them into five major groups. These groups have some overlap, and distinguish two types of energy medicine: <i>veritable</i> which involves scientifically observable <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Magnet_therapy" title="Magnet therapy">magnet therapy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colorpuncture" title="Colorpuncture">colorpuncture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Light_therapy" title="Light therapy">light therapy</a>) and <i>putative</i>, which invokes physically undetectable or unverifiable energy.<sup id="cite_ref-NCCIH_EMed_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIH_EMed-109">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>The NCCIH classification system is -</p>
<ol>
<li>Whole medical systems: Cut across more than one of the other groups; examples include traditional Chinese medicine, naturopathy, homeopathy, and ayurveda</li>
<li>Mind-body interventions: Explore the interconnection between the mind, body, and spirit, under the premise that the mind can affect "bodily functions and symptoms"</li>
<li>"Biology"-based practices: Use substances found in nature such as herbs, foods, vitamins, and other natural substances. (Note that as used here, "biology" does <i>not</i> refer to the science of <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, but is a usage newly coined by NCCIH in the primary source used for this article. "Biology-based" as coined by NCCIH may refer to chemicals from a nonbiological source, such as use of the poison <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> in traditional Chinese medicine, and to other nonbiological substances.)</li>
<li>Manipulative and body-based practices: feature manipulation or movement of body parts, such as is done in bodywork, chiropractic, and osteopathic manipulation</li>
<li>Energy medicine: is a domain that deals with putative and verifiable energy fields:
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Energy_(spirituality)#Biofields" class="mw-redirect" title="Energy (spirituality)">Biofield</a> therapies are intended to influence energy fields that are purported to surround and penetrate the body. No empirical evidence has been found to support the existence of the putative energy fields on which these therapies are predicated.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bioelectromagnetism" class="mw-redirect" title="Bioelectromagnetism">Bioelectromagnetic</a>-based therapies use verifiable electromagnetic fields, such as pulsed fields, alternating-current, or direct-current fields in an unconventional manner.</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ol>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_forms_of_alternative_medicine" title="List of forms of alternative medicine">List of forms of alternative medicine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pseudomedicine#List_of_fields_characterized_as_pseudomedicine" title="Pseudomedicine">Pseudomedicine §&#160;List of fields characterized as pseudomedicine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Xi%27an_traditionnal_medecine_market_(18).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Xi%27an_traditionnal_medecine_market_%2818%29.JPG/150px-Xi%27an_traditionnal_medecine_market_%2818%29.JPG" width="150" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Xi%27an_traditionnal_medecine_market_%2818%29.JPG/225px-Xi%27an_traditionnal_medecine_market_%2818%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Xi%27an_traditionnal_medecine_market_%2818%29.JPG/300px-Xi%27an_traditionnal_medecine_market_%2818%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Xi%27an_traditionnal_medecine_market_(18).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ready-to-drink <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">traditional Chinese medicine</a> mixture</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Alternative medicine consists of a wide range of health care practices, products, and therapies. The shared feature is a claim to heal that is not based on the scientific method. Alternative medicine practices are diverse in their foundations and methodologies.<sup id="cite_ref-NCCIH1_75-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIH1-75">[67]</a></sup> Alternative medicine practices may be classified by their cultural origins or by the types of beliefs upon which they are based.<sup id="cite_ref-NSF_2002_10-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSF_2002-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_16-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-16">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AMCER_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AMCER-26">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NCCIH1_75-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIH1-75">[67]</a></sup> Methods may incorporate or be based on traditional medicinal practices of a particular culture, folk knowledge, superstition,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO.27Connor1995.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DJXFPb88KLZQC.26pg.3DPA2_2.5D_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO.27Connor1995.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DJXFPb88KLZQC.26pg.3DPA2_2.5D-110">[98]</a></sup> spiritual beliefs, belief in <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a> energies (<a href="/wiki/Antiscience" title="Antiscience">antiscience</a>), <a href="/wiki/Pseudoscience" title="Pseudoscience">pseudoscience</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fallacy" title="Fallacy">errors in reasoning</a>, propaganda, fraud, new or different concepts of health and disease, and any bases other than being proven by scientific methods.<sup id="cite_ref-NSF_2002_10-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSF_2002-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_16-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-16">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hines_Sampson_Coulter_Sagan_17-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hines_Sampson_Coulter_Sagan-17">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AMCER_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AMCER-26">[20]</a></sup> Different cultures may have their own unique traditional or belief based practices developed recently or over thousands of years, and specific practices or entire systems of practices.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unscientific_belief_systems">Unscientific belief systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Unscientific belief systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Alternative medicine, such as using <a href="/wiki/Naturopathy" title="Naturopathy">naturopathy</a> or <a href="/wiki/Homeopathy" title="Homeopathy">homeopathy</a> in place of <a href="/wiki/Conventional_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Conventional medicine">conventional medicine</a>, is based on belief systems not grounded in science.<sup id="cite_ref-NCCIH1_75-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIH1-75">[67]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tr>
<th></th>
<th>Proposed mechanism</th>
<th>Issues</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<th><a href="/wiki/Naturopathy" title="Naturopathy">Naturopathy</a></th>
<td>Naturopathic medicine is based on a belief that the body heals itself using a supernatural <a href="/wiki/Vitalism" title="Vitalism">vital energy</a> that guides bodily processes.<sup id="cite_ref-SarrisWardle2010_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SarrisWardle2010-111">[99]</a></sup></td>
<td>In conflict with the paradigm of <a href="/wiki/Evidence-based_medicine" title="Evidence-based medicine">evidence-based medicine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Jagtenberg2006_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jagtenberg2006-112">[100]</a></sup> Many naturopaths have opposed <a href="/wiki/Vaccination" title="Vaccination">vaccination</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Ernst2001_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ernst2001-113">[101]</a></sup> and "scientific evidence does not support claims that naturopathic medicine can cure <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a> or any other disease".<sup id="cite_ref-ACS_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ACS-114">[102]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th><a href="/wiki/Homeopathy" title="Homeopathy">Homeopathy</a></th>
<td>A belief that a substance that causes the symptoms of a disease in healthy people cures similar symptoms in sick people.<sup id="cite_ref-Hahnemannquote_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hahnemannquote-116">[n 13]</a></sup></td>
<td>Developed before knowledge of <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a>, or of basic chemistry, which shows that repeated dilution as practiced in homeopathy produces only water, and that homeopathy is not scientifically valid.<sup id="cite_ref-Ernst2002_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ernst2002-117">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HCReport2010_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HCReport2010-118">[105]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-shang_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shang-119">[106]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NCCIH_homeopathy_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIH_homeopathy-120">[107]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditional_ethnic_systems">Traditional ethnic systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Traditional ethnic systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Alternative medical systems may be based on <a href="/wiki/Traditional_medicine" title="Traditional medicine">traditional medicine</a> practices, such as <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">traditional Chinese medicine</a> (TCM), <a href="/wiki/Ayurveda" title="Ayurveda">Ayurveda</a> in India, or practices of other cultures around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-NCCIH1_75-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIH1-75">[67]</a></sup> Some useful applications of traditional medicines have been researched and accepted within ordinary medicine, however the underlying belief systems are seldom scientific and are not accepted.</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tr>
<th></th>
<th>Claims</th>
<th>Issues</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">Traditional Chinese medicine</a></td>
<td>Traditional practices and beliefs from China, together with modifications made by the Communist party make up TCM. Common practices include herbal medicine, <a href="/wiki/Acupuncture" title="Acupuncture">acupuncture</a> (insertion of needles in the body at specified points), massage (Tui na), exercise (<a href="/wiki/Qigong" title="Qigong">qigong</a>), and dietary therapy.</td>
<td>The practices are based on belief in a supernatural energy called <a href="/wiki/Qi" title="Qi">qi</a>, considerations of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Astrology" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinese Astrology">Chinese Astrology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Numerology#Chinese_numerology" title="Numerology">Chinese numerology</a>, traditional use of herbs and other substances found in China—a belief that the tongue contains a map of the body that reflects changes in the body, and an incorrect model of the anatomy and physiology of internal organs.<sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_16-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-16">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BeyersteinSampson1996_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BeyersteinSampson1996-121">[108]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-LuNeedham1980_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LuNeedham1980-122">[109]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Maciocia1995_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maciocia1995-123">[110]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Matuk2006_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Matuk2006-124">[111]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Deshpande1987_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Deshpande1987-125">[112]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Ayurveda" title="Ayurveda">Ayurveda</a></td>
<td>Traditional medicine of India. Ayurveda believes in the existence of three elemental substances, the <a href="/wiki/Doshas" class="mw-redirect" title="Doshas">doshas</a> (called Vata, Pitta and Kapha), and states that a balance of the doshas results in health, while imbalance results in disease. Such disease-inducing imbalances can be adjusted and balanced using traditional herbs, minerals and heavy metals. Ayurveda stresses the use of plant-based medicines and treatments, with some animal products, and added minerals, including <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a>, lead, <a href="/wiki/Copper_sulfate" title="Copper sulfate">copper sulfate</a>.</td>
<td>Safety concerns have been raised about Ayurveda, with two U.S. studies finding about 20 percent of Ayurvedic Indian-manufactured <a href="/wiki/Patent_medicine" title="Patent medicine">patent medicines</a> contained toxic levels of heavy metals such as <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a>. Other concerns include the use of herbs containing toxic compounds and the lack of quality control in Ayurvedic facilities. Incidents of <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_poisoning" class="mw-redirect" title="Heavy metal poisoning">heavy metal poisoning</a> have been attributed to the use of these compounds in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWujastyk2003xviii_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWujastyk2003xviii-126">[113]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMishra20048_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMishra20048-127">[114]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DaguptaHStabler2011_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DaguptaHStabler2011-30">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Valiathan2006_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Valiathan2006-128">[115]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CDC_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CDC-129">[116]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Saper2008_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Saper2008-130">[117]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Supernatural_energies">Supernatural energies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Supernatural energies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Bases of belief may include belief in existence of supernatural energies undetected by the science of physics, as in biofields, or in belief in properties of the energies of physics that are inconsistent with the laws of physics, as in energy medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-NCCIH1_75-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIH1-75">[67]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tr>
<th></th>
<th>Claims</th>
<th>Issues</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Energy_medicine" title="Energy medicine">Biofield therapy</a></td>
<td>Intended to influence energy fields that, it is purported, surround and penetrate the body.<sup id="cite_ref-NCCIH1_75-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIH1-75">[67]</a></sup></td>
<td>Writers such as noted astrophysicist and advocate of skeptical thinking (<a href="/wiki/Scientific_skepticism" class="mw-redirect" title="Scientific skepticism">Scientific skepticism</a>) <a href="/wiki/Carl_Sagan" title="Carl Sagan">Carl Sagan</a> (1934-1996) have described the lack of empirical evidence to support the existence of the putative energy fields on which these therapies are predicated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESagan1996_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESagan1996-131">[118]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Bioelectromagnetism" class="mw-redirect" title="Bioelectromagnetism">Bioelectromagnetic</a> therapy</td>
<td>Use verifiable electromagnetic fields, such as pulsed fields, alternating-current, or direct-current fields in an unconventional manner.<sup id="cite_ref-NCCIH1_75-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIH1-75">[67]</a></sup></td>
<td>Asserts that magnets can be used to defy the laws of <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a> to influence health and disease.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Chiropractic" title="Chiropractic">Chiropractic</a></td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Spinal_manipulation" title="Spinal manipulation">Spinal manipulation</a> aims to treat "<a href="/wiki/Vertebral_subluxation" title="Vertebral subluxation">vertebral subluxations</a>" which are claimed to put pressure on nerves.</td>
<td>Chiropractic was developed in the belief that manipulating the spine affects the flow of a supernatural <a href="/wiki/Vital_energy" class="mw-redirect" title="Vital energy">vital energy</a> and thereby affects health and disease. Vertebral subluxation is a pseudoscientific concept and has not been proven to exist.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Reiki</td>
<td>Practitioners place their palms on the patient near <a href="/wiki/Chakras" class="mw-redirect" title="Chakras">Chakras</a> that they believe are centers of supernatural energies in the belief that these supernatural energies can transfer from the practitioner's palms to heal the patient.</td>
<td>Lacks credible scientific evidence.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[119]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Acupuncture1-1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Acupuncture1-1.jpg/220px-Acupuncture1-1.jpg" width="220" height="231" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Acupuncture1-1.jpg/330px-Acupuncture1-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Acupuncture1-1.jpg/440px-Acupuncture1-1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="525" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Acupuncture1-1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Acupuncture" title="Acupuncture">Acupuncture</a> involves insertion of needles in the body.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Holistic_therapy">Holistic therapy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Holistic therapy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="wikitable">
<tr>
<th></th>
<th>Claims</th>
<th>Issues</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Mind-body_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Mind-body medicine">Mind-body medicine</a></td>
<td>The mind can affect "bodily functions and symptoms" and there is an interconnection between the mind, body, and spirit.</td>
<td></td>
</tr>
</table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Herbal_remedies_and_other_substances_used">Herbal remedies and other substances used</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Herbal remedies and other substances used">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Phytotherapy" class="mw-redirect" title="Phytotherapy">Phytotherapy</a></div>
<p>Substance based practices use substances found in nature such as herbs, foods, non-vitamin supplements and megavitamins, animal and fungal products, and minerals, including use of these products in traditional medical practices that may also incorporate other methods.<sup id="cite_ref-NCCIH1_75-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIH1-75">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Abdulla1999_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abdulla1999-133">[120]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[121]</a></sup> Examples include healing claims for nonvitamin supplements, <a href="/wiki/Fish_oil" title="Fish oil">fish oil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Omega-3_fatty_acid" title="Omega-3 fatty acid">Omega-3 fatty acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glucosamine" title="Glucosamine">glucosamine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Echinacea" title="Echinacea">echinacea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flaxseed_oil" class="mw-redirect" title="Flaxseed oil">flaxseed oil</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ginseng" title="Ginseng">ginseng</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NCCIHSurvey_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIHSurvey-135">[122]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Herbal_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Herbal medicine">Herbal medicine</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Phytotherapy" class="mw-redirect" title="Phytotherapy">phytotherapy</a>, includes not just the use of plant products,
