but may also include the use of animal and mineral products.<sup id="cite_ref-Abdulla1999_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abdulla1999-133">[120]</a></sup> It is among the most commercially successful branches of alternative medicine, and includes the tablets, powders and elixirs that are sold as "nutritional supplements".<sup id="cite_ref-Abdulla1999_133-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abdulla1999-133">[120]</a></sup> Only a very small percentage of these have been shown to have any efficacy, and there is little regulation as to standards and safety of their contents.<sup id="cite_ref-Abdulla1999_133-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abdulla1999-133">[120]</a></sup> This may include use of known toxic substances, such as use of the poison <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> in traditional Chinese medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-NCCIHSurvey_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIHSurvey-135">[122]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chiropractic_spinal_adjustment.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Chiropractic_spinal_adjustment.jpg/220px-Chiropractic_spinal_adjustment.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Chiropractic_spinal_adjustment.jpg/330px-Chiropractic_spinal_adjustment.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Chiropractic_spinal_adjustment.jpg/440px-Chiropractic_spinal_adjustment.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2936" data-file-height="2202" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chiropractic_spinal_adjustment.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Chiropractor" class="mw-redirect" title="Chiropractor">chiropractor</a> "<a href="/wiki/Spinal_adjustment" title="Spinal adjustment">adjusting</a>" the spine.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion.2C_faith_healing.2C_and_prayer">Religion, faith healing, and prayer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Religion, faith healing, and prayer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="wikitable">
<tr>
<th></th>
<th>Claims</th>
<th>Issues</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">Christian</a> <a href="/wiki/Faith_healing" title="Faith healing">faith healing</a></td>
<td>There is a divine or spiritual intervention in healing.</td>
<td></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Shamanism" title="Shamanism">Shamanism</a></td>
<td>A practitioner can reach an <a href="/wiki/Altered_state_of_consciousness" title="Altered state of consciousness">altered states of consciousness</a> in order to encounter and interact with the spirit world or channel supernatural energies in the belief that they can heal.</td>
<td></td>
</tr>
</table>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/History_of_alternative_medicine" title="History of alternative medicine">history of alternative medicine</a> may refer to the history of a group of diverse medical practices that were collectively promoted as "alternative medicine" beginning in the 1970s, to the collection of individual histories of members of that group, or to the history of western medical practices that were labeled "irregular practices" by the western medical establishment.<sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_16-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-16">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-QMHCHF_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-QMHCHF-136">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CHBHAMA_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHBHAMA-137">[124]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NCHAMA_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCHAMA-138">[125]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RRCAM_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RRCAM-139">[126]</a></sup> It includes the histories of <a href="/wiki/Complementary_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Complementary medicine">complementary medicine</a> and of <a href="/wiki/Integrative_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Integrative medicine">integrative medicine</a>. Before the 1970s, western practitioners that were not part of the increasingly science-based medical establishment were referred to "irregular practitioners", and were dismissed by the medical establishment as unscientific and as practicing <a href="/wiki/Quackery" title="Quackery">quackery</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-QMHCHF_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-QMHCHF-136">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CHBHAMA_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHBHAMA-137">[124]</a></sup> Until the 1970's, irregular practice became increasingly marginalized as <a href="/wiki/Quackery" title="Quackery">quackery</a> and fraud, as western medicine increasingly incorporated scientific methods and discoveries, and had a corresponding increase in success of its treatments.<sup id="cite_ref-NCHAMA_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCHAMA-138">[125]</a></sup> In the 1970s, irregular practices were grouped with traditional practices of nonwestern cultures and with other unproven or disproven practices that were not part of biomedicine, with the entire group collectively marketed and promoted under the single expression "alternative medicine".<sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_16-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-16">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-QMHCHF_136-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-QMHCHF-136">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CHBHAMA_137-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHBHAMA-137">[124]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NCHAMA_138-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCHAMA-138">[125]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FrontlineAF_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FrontlineAF-140">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>Use of alternative medicine in the west began to rise following the <a href="/wiki/Counterculture_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Counterculture movement">counterculture movement</a> of the 1960s, as part of the rising <a href="/wiki/New_age_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="New age movement">new age movement</a> of the 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_16-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-16">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[128]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ENAB_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ENAB-142">[129]</a></sup> This was due to misleading <a href="/wiki/Mass_marketing" title="Mass marketing">mass marketing</a> of "alternative medicine" being an effective "alternative" to biomedicine, changing social attitudes about not using <a href="/wiki/Chemicals" class="mw-redirect" title="Chemicals">chemicals</a> and challenging <a href="/wiki/The_establishment" class="mw-redirect" title="The establishment">the establishment</a> and <a href="/wiki/Authority" title="Authority">authority</a> of any kind, sensitivity to giving equal measure to beliefs and practices of other cultures (<a href="/wiki/Cultural_relativism" title="Cultural relativism">cultural relativism</a>), and growing frustration and desperation by patients about limitations and <a href="/wiki/Side_effect" title="Side effect">side effects</a> of <a href="/wiki/Science-based_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Science-based medicine">science-based medicine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_16-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-16">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CHBHAMA_137-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHBHAMA-137">[124]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NCHAMA_138-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCHAMA-138">[125]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RRCAM_139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RRCAM-139">[126]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FrontlineAF_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FrontlineAF-140">[127]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ENAB_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ENAB-142">[129]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AMBMJ_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AMBMJ-143">[130]</a></sup> At the same time, in 1975, the <a href="/wiki/American_Medical_Association" title="American Medical Association">American Medical Association</a>, which played the central role in fighting quackery in the United States, abolished its quackery committee and closed down its Department of Investigation.<sup id="cite_ref-QMHCHF_136-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-QMHCHF-136">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:xxi</sup><sup id="cite_ref-AMBMJ_143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AMBMJ-143">[130]</a></sup> By the early to mid 1970s the expression "alternative medicine" came into widespread use, and the expression became mass marketed as a collection of "natural" and effective treatment "alternatives" to science-based biomedicine.<sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_16-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-16">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AMBMJ_143-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AMBMJ-143">[130]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[131]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[132]</a></sup> By 1983, mass marketing of "alternative medicine" was so pervasive that the <a href="/wiki/British_Medical_Journal" class="mw-redirect" title="British Medical Journal">British Medical Journal</a> (BMJ) pointed to "an apparently endless stream of books, articles, and radio and television programmes urge on the public the virtues of (alternative medicine) treatments ranging from meditation to drilling a hole in the skull to let in more oxygen".<sup id="cite_ref-AMBMJ_143-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AMBMJ-143">[130]</a></sup> In this 1983 article, the BMJ wrote, "one of the few growth industries in contemporary Britain is alternative medicine", noting that by 1983, "33% of patients with rheumatoid arthritis and 39% of those with backache admitted to having consulted an alternative practitioner".<sup id="cite_ref-AMBMJ_143-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AMBMJ-143">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>By about 1990, the American alternative medicine industry had grown to a $27 billion per year, with polls showing 30% of Americans were using it.<sup id="cite_ref-ENAB_142-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ENAB-142">[129]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PAMNIH_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PAMNIH-146">[133]</a></sup> Moreover, polls showed that Americans made more visits for alternative therapies than the total number of visits to primary care doctors, and American out-of-pocket spending (non-insurance spending) on alternative medicine was about equal to spending on biomedical doctors.<sup id="cite_ref-QMHCHF_136-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-QMHCHF-136">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:172</sup> In 1991, <a href="/wiki/Time_magazine" class="mw-redirect" title="Time magazine">Time magazine</a> ran a cover story, "The New Age of Alternative Medicine: Why New Age Medicine Is Catching On".<sup id="cite_ref-ENAB_142-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ENAB-142">[129]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PAMNIH_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PAMNIH-146">[133]</a></sup> In 1993, the <a href="/wiki/New_England_Journal_of_Medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="New England Journal of Medicine">New England Journal of Medicine</a> reported one in three Americans as using alternative medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-ENAB_142-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ENAB-142">[129]</a></sup> In 1993, the <a href="/wiki/Public_Broadcasting_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Public Broadcasting System">Public Broadcasting System</a> ran a <a href="/wiki/Bill_Moyers" title="Bill Moyers">Bill Moyers</a> special, <a href="/wiki/Healing_and_the_Mind" class="mw-redirect" title="Healing and the Mind">Healing and the Mind</a>, with Moyers commenting that "...people by the tens of millions are using alternative medicine. If established medicine does not understand that, they are going to lose their clients."<sup id="cite_ref-ENAB_142-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ENAB-142">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another explosive growth began in the 1990s, when senior level political figures began promoting alternative medicine, investing large sums of government medical research funds into testing alternative medicine, including testing of scientifically implausible treatments, and relaxing government regulation of alternative medicine products as compared to biomedical products.<sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_16-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-16">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-QMHCHF_136-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-QMHCHF-136">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:xxi</sup><sup id="cite_ref-CHBHAMA_137-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHBHAMA-137">[124]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NCHAMA_138-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCHAMA-138">[125]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RRCAM_139-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RRCAM-139">[126]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FrontlineAF_140-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FrontlineAF-140">[127]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TAMUUS_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TAMUUS-147">[134]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TT_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TT-148">[135]</a></sup> Beginning with a 1991 appropriation of $2 million for funding research of alternative medicine research, federal spending grew to a cumulative total of about $2.5 billion by 2009, with 50% of Americans using alternative medicine by 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-.242.5_billion_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-.242.5_billion-149">[136]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BRAUSAToday_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BRAUSAToday-150">[137]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1993, Britain's <a href="/wiki/Prince_Charles" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince Charles">Prince Charles</a>, who claimed that homeopathy and other alternative medicine was an effective alternative to biomedicine, established <a href="/wiki/The_Prince%27s_Foundation_for_Integrated_Health" title="The Prince's Foundation for Integrated Health">The Prince's Foundation for Integrated Health</a> (FIH), as a charity to explore "how safe, proven complementary therapies can work in conjunction with mainstream medicine".<sup id="cite_ref-Booth_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Booth-151">[138]</a></sup> The FIH received government funding through grants from Britain's <a href="/wiki/Department_of_Health_(United_Kingdom)" title="Department of Health (United Kingdom)">Department of Health</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Booth_151-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Booth-151">[138]</a></sup> In 2008, London's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> published a letter from <a href="/wiki/Edzard_Ernst" title="Edzard Ernst">Edzard Ernst</a> that asked the FIH to recall two guides promoting alternative medicine, saying: "the majority of alternative therapies appear to be clinically ineffective, and many are downright dangerous." In 2010, the FIH closed after allegations of fraud and money laundering led to arrests of its officials.<sup id="cite_ref-Booth_151-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Booth-151">[138]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2001, <a href="/wiki/MEDLINE" title="MEDLINE">MEDLINE</a> introduced a <a href="/wiki/MeSH" class="mw-redirect" title="MeSH">MeSH</a> term to index scientific publications in alternative medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[139]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[140]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2004, modifications of the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament" title="European Parliament">European Parliament</a>'s 2001 <a href="/wiki/Directive_2001/83/EC" title="Directive 2001/83/EC">Directive 2001/83/EC</a>, regulating all medicine products, were made with the expectation of influencing development of the European market for alternative medicine products.<sup id="cite_ref-RFCAME_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RFCAME-154">[141]</a></sup> Regulation of alternative medicine in Europe was loosened with "a simplified registration procedure" for traditional herbal medicinal products.<sup id="cite_ref-RFCAME_154-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RFCAME-154">[141]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-D200183EC_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-D200183EC-155">[142]</a></sup> Plausible "efficacy" for traditional medicine was redefined to be based on long term popularity and testimonials ("the pharmacological effects or efficacy of the medicinal product are plausible on the basis of long-standing use and experience."), without scientific testing.<sup id="cite_ref-RFCAME_154-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RFCAME-154">[141]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-D200183EC_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-D200183EC-155">[142]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Committee_on_Herbal_Medicinal_Products" title="Committee on Herbal Medicinal Products">Committee on Herbal Medicinal Products</a> (HMPC) was created within the <a href="/wiki/European_Medicines_Agency" title="European Medicines Agency">European Medicines Agency</a> in London (EMEA). A special working group was established for homeopathic remedies under the <a href="/wiki/Heads_of_Medicines_Agencies" title="Heads of Medicines Agencies">Heads of Medicines Agencies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-RFCAME_154-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RFCAME-154">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>Through 2004, alternative medicine that was traditional to Germany continued to be a regular part of the health care system, including <a href="/wiki/Homeopathy" title="Homeopathy">homeopathy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anthroposophic_medicine" title="Anthroposophic medicine">anthroposophic medicine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-RFCAME_154-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RFCAME-154">[141]</a></sup> The <a href="/w/index.php?title=German_Medicines_Act&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="German Medicines Act (page does not exist)">German Medicines Act</a> mandated that science-based medical authorities consider the "particular characteristics" of complementary and alternative medicines.<sup id="cite_ref-RFCAME_154-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RFCAME-154">[141]</a></sup> By 2004, homeopathy had grown to be the most used alternative therapy in France, growing from 16% of the population using homeopathic medicine in 1982, to 29% by 1987, 36% percent by 1992, and 62% of French mothers using homeopathic medicines by 2004, with 94.5% of French pharmacists advising pregnant women to use homeopathic remedies.<sup id="cite_ref-HME1_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HME1-156">[143]</a></sup> As of 2004<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, 100 million people in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> depended solely on traditional German homeopathic remedies for their medical care.<sup id="cite_ref-HBI_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBI-157">[144]</a></sup> As of 2010<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, homeopathic remedies continued to be the leading alternative treatment used by European physicians.<sup id="cite_ref-HME1_156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HME1-156">[143]</a></sup> By 2005, sales of <a href="/wiki/Homeopathic_remedies" class="mw-redirect" title="Homeopathic remedies">homeopathic remedies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anthroposophical_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Anthroposophical medicine">anthroposophical medicine</a> had grown to $930 million Euros, a 60% increase from 1995.<sup id="cite_ref-HME1_156-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HME1-156">[143]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[145]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since 2009, according to Art. 118a of the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Federal_Constitution" title="Swiss Federal Constitution">Swiss Federal Constitution</a>, the Swiss Confederation and the <a href="/wiki/Cantons_of_Switzerland" title="Cantons of Switzerland">Cantons of Switzerland</a> shall within the scope of their powers ensure that consideration is given to complementary medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 2013, 50% of Americans were using CAM.<sup id="cite_ref-BRAUSAToday_150-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BRAUSAToday-150">[137]</a></sup> As of 2013<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, CAM medicinal products in Europe continued to be exempted from documented efficacy standards required of other medicinal products.<sup id="cite_ref-LSRCAME_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LSRCAME-160">[147]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Individual_systems_and_practices">Individual systems and practices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Individual systems and practices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Much of what is now categorized as alternative medicine was developed as independent, complete medical systems. These were developed long before biomedicine and use of scientific methods. Each system was developed in relatively isolated regions of the world where there was little or no medical contact with pre-scientific western medicine, or with each other's systems. Examples are <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">traditional Chinese medicine</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ayurvedic_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Ayurvedic medicine">Ayurvedic medicine</a> of <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>.</p>
<p>Other alternative medicine practices, such as <a href="/wiki/Homeopathy" title="Homeopathy">homeopathy</a>, were developed in western Europe and in opposition to western medicine, at a time when western medicine was based on unscientific theories that were dogmatically imposed by western religious authorities. Homeopathy was developed prior to discovery of the basic principles of <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a>, which proved homeopathic remedies contained nothing but water. But homeopathy, with its remedies made of water, was harmless compared to the unscientific and dangerous orthodox western medicine practiced at that time, which included use of <a href="/wiki/Toxin" title="Toxin">toxins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bloodletting" title="Bloodletting">draining of blood</a>, often resulting in permanent disfigurement or death.<sup id="cite_ref-CHBHAMA_137-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHBHAMA-137">[124]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other alternative practices such as <a href="/wiki/Chiropractic" title="Chiropractic">chiropractic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Osteopathic_manipulative_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Osteopathic manipulative medicine">osteopathic manipulative medicine</a> were developed in the United States at a time that western medicine was beginning to incorporate scientific methods and theories, but the biomedical model was not yet totally dominant. Practices such as chiropractic and osteopathic, each considered to be irregular practices by the western medical establishment, also opposed each other, both rhetorically and politically with licensing legislation. Osteopathic practitioners added the courses and training of biomedicine to their licensing, and licensed Doctor of Osteopathic Medicine holders began diminishing use of the unscientific origins of the field. Without the original nonscientific practices and theories, osteopathic medicine is now considered the same as biomedicine.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id=".22Irregular_practitioners.22">"Irregular practitioners"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: &quot;Irregular practitioners&quot;">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_alternative_medicine" title="History of alternative medicine">History of alternative medicine</a></div>
<p><i>Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_medicine" title="History of medicine">Rise of modern medicine</a></i></p>
<p>Until the 1970s, western practitioners that were not part of the medical establishment were referred to "irregular practitioners", and were dismissed by the medical establishment as unscientific, as practicing <a href="/wiki/Quackery" title="Quackery">quackery</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CHBHAMA_137-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHBHAMA-137">[124]</a></sup> The Irregular practice became increasingly marginalized as <a href="/wiki/Quackery" title="Quackery">quackery</a> and fraud, as western medicine increasingly incorporated scientific methods and discoveries, and had a corresponding increase in success of its treatments.</p>
<p>Dating from the 1970s, medical professionals, sociologists, anthropologists and other commentators noted the increasing visibility of a wide variety of health practices that had neither derived directly from nor been verified by <a href="/wiki/Medical_research" title="Medical research">biomedical science</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bivins2011_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bivins2011-161">[148]</a></sup> Since that time, those who have analyzed this trend have deliberated over the most apt language with which to describe this emergent health field.<sup id="cite_ref-Bivins2011_161-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bivins2011-161">[148]</a></sup> A variety of terms have been used, including heterodox, irregular, fringe and alternative medicine while others, particularly medical commentators, have been satisfied to label them as instances of quackery.<sup id="cite_ref-Bivins2011_161-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bivins2011-161">[148]</a></sup> The most persistent term has been alternative medicine but its use is problematic as it assumes a value-laden dichotomy between a medical fringe, implicitly of borderline acceptability at best, and a privileged medical orthodoxy, associated with validated medico-scientific norms.<sup id="cite_ref-Jutte2001_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jutte2001-162">[149]</a></sup> The use of the category of alternative medicine has also been criticized as it cannot be studied as an independent entity but must be understood in terms of a regionally and temporally specific medical orthodoxy.<sup id="cite_ref-DeBlecourt1999_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeBlecourt1999-163">[150]</a></sup> Its use can also be misleading as it may erroneously imply that a real medical alternative exists.<sup id="cite_ref-Jutte1999_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jutte1999-164">[151]</a></sup> As with near-synonymous expressions, such as unorthodox, complementary, marginal, or quackery, these linguistic devices have served, in the context of processes of professionalisation and market competition, to establish the authority of official medicine and police the boundary between it and its unconventional rivals.<sup id="cite_ref-Jutte2001_162-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jutte2001-162">[149]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Peking_Union_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Peking_Union_1.jpg/220px-Peking_Union_1.jpg" width="220" height="111" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Peking_Union_1.jpg/330px-Peking_Union_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Peking_Union_1.jpg/440px-Peking_Union_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1311" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Peking_Union_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Peking_Union_Medical_College" title="Peking Union Medical College">Peking Union Medical College</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>An early instance of the influence of this modern, or western, scientific medicine outside Europe and North America is <a href="/wiki/Peking_Union_Medical_College" title="Peking Union Medical College">Peking Union Medical College</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bullock1980_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bullock1980-165">[152]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[n 14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[n 15]</a></sup></p>
<p>From a historical perspective, the emergence of alternative medicine, if not the term itself, is typically dated to the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Porter_Ramsey_Loudon_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Porter_Ramsey_Loudon-168">[153]</a></sup> This is despite the fact that there are variants of Western non-conventional medicine that arose in the late-eighteenth century or earlier and some non-Western medical traditions, currently considered alternative in the West and elsewhere, which boast extended historical pedigrees.<sup id="cite_ref-Jutte2001_162-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jutte2001-162">[149]</a></sup> Alternative medical systems, however, can only be said to exist when there is an identifiable, regularized and authoritative standard medical practice, such as arose in the West during the nineteenth century, to which they can function as an alternative.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBivins2007171_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBivins2007171-169">[154]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the late eighteenth and nineteenth centuries regular and irregular medical practitioners became more clearly differentiated throughout much of Europe and,<sup id="cite_ref-Brunton_Brown_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brunton_Brown-170">[155]</a></sup> as the nineteenth century progressed, most Western states converged in the creation of legally delimited and semi-protected medical markets.<sup id="cite_ref-Pickstone2006_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pickstone2006-171">[156]</a></sup> It is at this point that an "official" medicine, created in cooperation with the state and employing a scientific rhetoric of <a href="/wiki/Legitimation" title="Legitimation">legitimacy</a>, emerges as a recognizable entity and that the concept of alternative medicine as a historical category becomes tenable.<sup id="cite_ref-Ramsey1999_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ramsey1999-172">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>As part of this process, professional adherents of mainstream medicine in countries such as Germany, France, and Britain increasingly invoked the scientific basis of their discipline as a means of engendering internal professional unity and of external differentiation in the face of sustained market competition from homeopaths, naturopaths, mesmerists and other nonconventional medical practitioners, finally achieving a degree of imperfect dominance through alliance with the state and the passage of regulatory legislation.<sup id="cite_ref-Jutte2001_162-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jutte2001-162">[149]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jutte1999_164-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jutte1999-164">[151]</a></sup> In the US the <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_School_of_Medicine" title="Johns Hopkins School of Medicine">Johns Hopkins University School of Medicine</a>, based in Baltimore, Maryland, opened in 1893, with <a href="/wiki/William_H._Welch" title="William H. Welch">William H. Welch</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Osler" title="William Osler">William Osler</a> among the founding physicians, and was the first medical school devoted to teaching "German scientific medicine".<sup id="cite_ref-Silverman2011_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Silverman2011-173">[158]</a></sup></p>
<p>Buttressed by increased authority arising from significant advances in the medical sciences of the late 19th century onwards—including development and application of the <a href="/wiki/Germ_theory_of_disease" title="Germ theory of disease">germ theory of disease</a> by the chemist <a href="/wiki/Louis_Pasteur" title="Louis Pasteur">Louis Pasteur</a> and the surgeon <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Lister,_1st_Baron_Lister" class="mw-redirect" title="Joseph Lister, 1st Baron Lister">Joseph Lister</a>, of <a href="/wiki/Microbiology" title="Microbiology">microbiology</a> co-founded by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Koch" title="Robert Koch">Robert Koch</a> (in 1885 appointed professor of <a href="/wiki/Hygiene" title="Hygiene">hygiene</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Berlin" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Berlin">University of Berlin</a>), and of the use of <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-rays</a> (<a href="/wiki/R%C3%B6ntgen_ray" class="mw-redirect" title="Röntgen ray">Röntgen rays</a>)—the 1910 <a href="/wiki/Flexner_Report" title="Flexner Report">Flexner Report</a> called upon <a href="/wiki/Medical_school_in_the_United_States" title="Medical school in the United States">American medical schools</a> to follow the model of the Johns Hopkins School of Medicine, and adhere to mainstream science in their teaching and research. This was in a belief, mentioned in the Report's introduction, that the preliminary and professional training then prevailing in medical schools should be reformed, in view of the new means for diagnosing and combating disease made available the sciences on which medicine depended.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[n 16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hiatt2003_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hiatt2003-176">[160]</a></sup></p>
<p>Putative medical practices at the time that later became known as "alternative medicine" included homeopathy (founded in Germany in the early 19c.) and chiropractic (founded in North America in the late 19c.). These conflicted in principle with the developments in medical science upon which the Flexner reforms were based, and they have not become compatible with further advances of medical science such as listed in <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_medicine_and_medical_technology" title="Timeline of medicine and medical technology">Timeline of medicine and medical technology</a>, 1900–1999 and 2000–present, nor have Ayurveda, acupuncture or other kinds of alternative medicine.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>At the same time "<a href="/wiki/Tropical_medicine" title="Tropical medicine">Tropical medicine</a>" was being developed as a specialist branch of western medicine in research establishments such as <a href="/wiki/Liverpool_School_of_Tropical_Medicine" title="Liverpool School of Tropical Medicine">Liverpool School of Tropical Medicine</a> founded in 1898 by <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Lewis_Jones" title="Alfred Lewis Jones">Alfred Lewis Jones</a>, <a href="/wiki/London_School_of_Hygiene_%26_Tropical_Medicine" title="London School of Hygiene &amp; Tropical Medicine">London School of Hygiene &amp; Tropical Medicine</a>, founded in 1899 by <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Manson" title="Patrick Manson">Patrick Manson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tulane_University_School_of_Public_Health_and_Tropical_Medicine" title="Tulane University School of Public Health and Tropical Medicine">Tulane University School of Public Health and Tropical Medicine</a>, instituted in 1912. A distinction was being made between western scientific medicine and indigenous systems. An example is given by an official report about indigenous systems of medicine in India, including Ayurveda, submitted by <a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Usman_of_Madras" title="Mohammad Usman of Madras">Mohammad Usman of Madras</a> and others in 1923. This stated that the first question the Committee considered was "to decide whether the indigenous systems of medicine were scientific or not".<sup id="cite_ref-Usman1925_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Usman1925-177">[161]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Madras1984_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Madras1984-178">[162]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBivins2007164.E2.80.93170_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBivins2007164.E2.80.93170-179">[163]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the later twentieth century the term 'alternative medicine' entered public discourse,<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[n 17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BMA_Cant_Saks_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BMA_Cant_Saks-182">[165]</a></sup> but it was not always being used with the same meaning by all parties. <a href="/wiki/Arnold_S._Relman" title="Arnold S. Relman">Arnold S. Relman</a> remarked in 1998 that in the best kind of medical practice, all proposed treatments must be tested objectively, and that in the end there will only be treatments that pass and those that do not, those that are proven worthwhile and those that are not. He asked 'Can there be any reasonable "alternative"?'<sup id="cite_ref-Relman1998_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Relman1998-183">[166]</a></sup> But also in 1998 the then <a href="/wiki/Surgeon_General_of_the_United_States" title="Surgeon General of the United States">Surgeon General of the United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Satcher" title="David Satcher">David Satcher</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-HistoryMakers_HHS_Satcher_Bio_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HistoryMakers_HHS_Satcher_Bio-184">[167]</a></sup> issued public information about eight common alternative treatments (including acupuncture, holistic and massage), together with information about common diseases and conditions, on nutrition, diet, and lifestyle changes, and about helping consumers to decipher fraud and quackery, and to find healthcare centers and doctors who practiced alternative medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-New_Millennium_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-New_Millennium-185">[168]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 1990, approximately 60 million Americans had used one or more complementary or alternative therapies to address health issues, according to a nationwide survey in the US published in 1993 by David Eisenberg.<sup id="cite_ref-Eisenberg1993_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eisenberg1993-186">[169]</a></sup> A study published in the November 11, 1998 issue of the <a href="/wiki/Journal_of_the_American_Medical_Association" class="mw-redirect" title="Journal of the American Medical Association">Journal of the American Medical Association</a> reported that 42% of Americans had used complementary and alternative therapies, up from 34% in 1990.<sup id="cite_ref-Eisenberg1998_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eisenberg1998-187">[170]</a></sup> However, despite the growth in patient demand for complementary medicine, most of the early alternative/complementary medical centers failed.<sup id="cite_ref-Bravewell2007_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bravewell2007-188">[171]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical_education">Medical education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Medical education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Mainly as a result of reforms following the Flexner Report of 1910<sup id="cite_ref-Flexner_Report_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flexner_Report-189">[172]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Medical_education" title="Medical education">medical education</a> in established <a href="/wiki/Medical_schools" class="mw-redirect" title="Medical schools">medical schools</a> in the US has generally not included alternative medicine as a teaching topic.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[n 18]</a></sup> Typically, their teaching is based on current practice and scientific knowledge about: anatomy, physiology, histology, embryology, neuroanatomy, pathology, pharmacology, microbiology and immunology.<sup id="cite_ref-Bianco_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bianco-192">[174]</a></sup> Medical schools' teaching includes such topics as doctor-patient communication, ethics, the art of medicine,<sup id="cite_ref-Stanford_Curriculum_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stanford_Curriculum-193">[175]</a></sup> and engaging in complex clinical reasoning (medical decision-making).<sup id="cite_ref-Yale_Curriculum_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yale_Curriculum-194">[176]</a></sup> Writing in 2002, Snyderman and Weil remarked that by the early twentieth century the Flexner model had helped to create the 20th-century academic health center, in which education, research, and practice were inseparable. While this had much improved medical practice by defining with increasing certainty the <a href="/wiki/Pathophysiological" class="mw-redirect" title="Pathophysiological">pathophysiological</a> basis of disease, a single-minded focus on the pathophysiological had diverted much of mainstream American medicine from clinical conditions that were not well understood in mechanistic terms, and were not effectively treated by conventional therapies.<sup id="cite_ref-Snyderman2002_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snyderman2002-195">[177]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 2001 some form of CAM training was being offered by at least 75 out of 125 medical schools in the US.<sup id="cite_ref-Berman2001_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berman2001-196">[178]</a></sup> Exceptionally, the School of Medicine of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Maryland,_Baltimore" title="University of Maryland, Baltimore">University of Maryland, Baltimore</a> includes a research institute for integrative medicine (a member entity of the Cochrane Collaboration).<sup id="cite_ref-UM_CAM_Field_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UM_CAM_Field-108">[96]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UM_CIM_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UM_CIM-197">[179]</a></sup> Medical schools are responsible for conferring medical degrees, but a physician typically may not legally practice medicine until licensed by the local government authority. Licensed <a href="/wiki/Physicians_in_the_United_States" title="Physicians in the United States">physicians in the US</a> who have attended one of the established medical schools there have usually graduated Doctor of Medicine (MD).<sup id="cite_ref-Medline_MD_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Medline_MD-198">[180]</a></sup> All states require that applicants for MD licensure be graduates of an approved medical school and complete the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Medical_Licensing_Examination" title="United States Medical Licensing Examination">United States Medical Licensing Exam (USMLE)</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Medline_MD_198-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Medline_MD-198">[180]</a></sup></p>
<p>The British Medical Association, in its publication <i>Complementary Medicine, New Approach to Good Practice</i> (1993), gave as a working definition of non-conventional therapies (including acupuncture, chiropractic and homeopathy): "...those forms of treatment which are not widely used by the orthodox health-care professions, and the skills of which are not part of the undergraduate curriculum of orthodox medical and paramedical health-care courses."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBMA1993_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBMA1993-199">[181]</a></sup> By 2000 some medical schools in the UK were offering CAM familiarisation courses to undergraduate medical students while some were also offering modules specifically on CAM.<sup id="cite_ref-Walton_Science_and_Technology_Committee_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walton_Science_and_Technology_Committee-200">[182]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States_government">United States government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: United States government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/National_Center_for_Complementary_and_Integrative_Health" title="National Center for Complementary and Integrative Health">National Center for Complementary and Integrative Health</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HARKINEAT.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/36/HARKINEAT.jpg/220px-HARKINEAT.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/36/HARKINEAT.jpg/330px-HARKINEAT.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/3/36/HARKINEAT.jpg 2x" data-file-width="410" data-file-height="273" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HARKINEAT.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sen. <a href="/wiki/Tom_Harkin" title="Tom Harkin">Tom Harkin</a> at a press conference.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1991, pointing to a need for testing because of the widespread use of alternative medicine without authoritative information on its efficacy, United States Senator <a href="/wiki/Tom_Harkin" title="Tom Harkin">Tom Harkin</a> used $2 million of his discretionary funds to create the <a href="/wiki/Office_for_the_Study_of_Unconventional_Medical_Practices" class="mw-redirect" title="Office for the Study of Unconventional Medical Practices">Office for the Study of Unconventional Medical Practices</a> (OSUMP), later renamed to be the <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Alternative_Medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Office of Alternative Medicine">Office of Alternative Medicine</a> (OAM).<sup id="cite_ref-QMHCHF_136-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-QMHCHF-136">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:170</sup><sup id="cite_ref-NC_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NC-201">[183]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NCCAME_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCAME-202">[184]</a></sup> The OAM was created to be within the <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_of_Health" class="mw-redirect" title="National Institute of Health">National Institute of Health</a> (NIH), the scientifically prestigious primary agency of the United States government responsible for biomedical and health-related research.<sup id="cite_ref-QMHCHF_136-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-QMHCHF-136">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:170</sup><sup id="cite_ref-NC_201-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NC-201">[183]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NCCAME_202-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCAME-202">[184]</a></sup> The mandate was to investigate, evaluate, and validate effective alternative medicine treatments, and alert the public as the results of testing its efficacy.<sup id="cite_ref-PAMNIH_146-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PAMNIH-146">[133]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NC_201-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NC-201">[183]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NCCAME_202-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCAME-202">[184]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[185]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sen. Harkin had become convinced his allergies were cured by taking <a href="/wiki/Bee_pollen" title="Bee pollen">bee pollen</a> pills, and was urged to make the spending by two of his influential constituents.<sup id="cite_ref-PAMNIH_146-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PAMNIH-146">[133]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NC_201-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NC-201">[183]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NCCAME_202-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCAME-202">[184]</a></sup> Bedell, a longtime friend of Sen. Harkin, was a former member of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">United States House of Representatives</a> who believed that alternative medicine had twice cured him of diseases after mainstream medicine had failed, claiming that cow's milk <a href="/wiki/Colostrum" title="Colostrum">colostrum</a> cured his <a href="/wiki/Lyme_disease" title="Lyme disease">Lyme disease</a>, and an herbal derivative from <a href="/wiki/Camphor" title="Camphor">camphor</a> had prevented post surgical recurrence of his <a href="/wiki/Prostate_cancer" title="Prostate cancer">prostate cancer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-QMHCHF_136-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-QMHCHF-136">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PAMNIH_146-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PAMNIH-146">[133]</a></sup> Wiewel was a promoter of unproven cancer treatments involving a mixture of blood sera that the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">Food and Drug Administration</a> had banned from being imported.<sup id="cite_ref-PAMNIH_146-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PAMNIH-146">[133]</a></sup> Both Bedell and Wiewel became members of the advisory panel for the OAM. The company that sold the bee pollen was later fined by the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Trade_Commission" title="Federal Trade Commission">Federal Trade Commission</a> for making false health claims about their bee-pollen products reversing the aging process, curing allergies, and helping with weight loss.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[186]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1994, Sen. Harkin (D) and Senator <a href="/wiki/Orrin_Hatch" title="Orrin Hatch">Orrin Hatch</a> (R) introduced the <a href="/wiki/Dietary_Supplement_Health_and_Education_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Dietary Supplement Health and Education Act">Dietary Supplement Health and Education Act</a> (<a href="/wiki/DSHEA" class="mw-redirect" title="DSHEA">DSHEA</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-NCDLP2000_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCDLP2000-205">[187]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-1993SOPA_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1993SOPA-206">[188]</a></sup> The act reduced authority of the FDA to monitor products sold as "natural" treatments.<sup id="cite_ref-NCDLP2000_205-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCDLP2000-205">[187]</a></sup> Labeling standards were reduced to allow health claims for supplements based only on unconfirmed preliminary studies that were not subjected to scientific peer review, and the act made it more difficult for the FDA to promptly seize products or demand proof of safety where there was evidence of a product being dangerous.<sup id="cite_ref-1993SOPA_206-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1993SOPA-206">[188]</a></sup> The Act became known as the "<a href="/wiki/The_1993_Snake_Oil_Protection_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="The 1993 Snake Oil Protection Act">The 1993 Snake Oil Protection Act</a>" following a <a href="/wiki/New_York_Times" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Times">New York Times</a> editorial under that name.<sup id="cite_ref-NCDLP2000_205-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCDLP2000-205">[187]</a></sup></p>
<p>Senator Harkin complained about the "unbendable rules of <a href="/wiki/Randomized_clinical_trials" class="mw-redirect" title="Randomized clinical trials">randomized clinical trials</a>", citing his use of bee pollen to treat his allergies, which he claimed to be effective even though it was biologically implausible and efficacy was not established using scientific methods.<sup id="cite_ref-NC_201-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NC-201">[183]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SMPCAT_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SMPCAT-207">[189]</a></sup> Sen. Harkin asserted that claims for alternative medicine efficacy be allowed not only without conventional scientific testing, even when they are biologically implausible, "It is not necessary for the scientific community to understand the process before the American public can benefit from these therapies."<sup id="cite_ref-NCDLP2000_205-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCDLP2000-205">[187]</a></sup> Following passage of the act, sales rose from about $4 billion in 1994, to $20 billion by the end of 2000, at the same time as evidence of their lack of efficacy or harmful effects grew.<sup id="cite_ref-NCDLP2000_205-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCDLP2000-205">[187]</a></sup> Senator Harkin came into open public conflict with the first OAM Director <a href="/wiki/Joseph_M._Jacobs" class="mw-redirect" title="Joseph M. Jacobs">Joseph M. Jacobs</a> and OAM board members from the scientific and biomedical community.<sup id="cite_ref-NCCAME_202-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCAME-202">[184]</a></sup> Jacobs' insistence on rigorous scientific methodology caused friction with Senator Harkin.<sup id="cite_ref-NC_201-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NC-201">[183]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SMPCAT_207-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SMPCAT-207">[189]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[190]</a></sup> Increasing political resistance to the use of scientific methodology was publicly criticized by Dr. Jacobs and another OAM board member complained that "nonsense has trickled down to every aspect of this office...It's the only place where opinions are counted as equal to data."<sup id="cite_ref-NC_201-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NC-201">[183]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SMPCAT_207-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SMPCAT-207">[189]</a></sup> In 1994, Senator Harkin appeared on television with cancer patients who blamed Dr. Jacobs for blocking their access to untested cancer treatment, leading Jacobs to resign in frustration.<sup id="cite_ref-NC_201-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NC-201">[183]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SMPCAT_207-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SMPCAT-207">[189]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1995, <a href="/wiki/Wayne_Jonas" title="Wayne Jonas">Wayne Jonas</a>, a promoter of <a href="/wiki/Homeopathy" title="Homeopathy">homeopathy</a> and political ally of Senator Harkin, became the director of the OAM, and continued in that role until 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[191]</a></sup> In 1997, the NCCAM budget was increased from $12 million to $20 million annually.<sup id="cite_ref-OAMGUB_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OAMGUB-210">[192]</a></sup> From 1990 to 1997, use of alternative medicine in the US increased by 25%, with a corresponding 50% increase in expenditures.<sup id="cite_ref-Eisenberg1998_187-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eisenberg1998-187">[170]</a></sup> The OAM drew increasing criticism from eminent members of the scientific community with letters to the Senate Appropriations Committee when discussion of renewal of funding OAM came up.<sup id="cite_ref-QMHCHF_136-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-QMHCHF-136">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:175</sup> Nobel laureate <a href="/wiki/Paul_Berg" title="Paul Berg">Paul Berg</a> wrote that prestigious NIH should not be degraded to act as a cover for quackery, calling the OAM "an embarrassment to serious scientists."<sup id="cite_ref-QMHCHF_136-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-QMHCHF-136">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:175</sup><sup id="cite_ref-OAMGUB_210-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OAMGUB-210">[192]</a></sup> The president of the <a href="/wiki/American_Physical_Society" title="American Physical Society">American Physical Society</a> wrote complaining that the government was spending money on testing products and practices that "violate basic laws of physics and more clearly resemble witchcraft".<sup id="cite_ref-QMHCHF_136-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-QMHCHF-136">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:175</sup><sup id="cite_ref-OAMGUB_210-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OAMGUB-210">[192]</a></sup> In 1998, the President of the North Carolina Medical Association publicly called for shutting down the OAM.<sup id="cite_ref-WNCCAMSBD_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WNCCAMSBD-211">[193]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1998, NIH director and Nobel laureate <a href="/wiki/Harold_Varmus" class="mw-redirect" title="Harold Varmus">Harold Varmus</a> came into conflict with Senator Harkin by pushing to have more NIH control of alternative medicine research.<sup id="cite_ref-SSOAGGRAM_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SSOAGGRAM-212">[194]</a></sup> The NIH Director placed the OAM under more strict scientific NIH control.<sup id="cite_ref-OAMGUB_210-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OAMGUB-210">[192]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SSOAGGRAM_212-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SSOAGGRAM-212">[194]</a></sup> Senator Harkin responded by elevating OAM into an independent NIH "center", just short of being its own "institute", and renamed to be the <a href="/wiki/National_Center_for_Complementary_and_Alternative_Medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="National Center for Complementary and Alternative Medicine">National Center for Complementary and Alternative Medicine</a> (NCCAM). NCCAM had a mandate to promote a more rigorous and scientific approach to the study of alternative medicine, research training and career development, outreach, and "integration". In 1999, the NCCAM budget was increased from $20 million to $50 million.<sup id="cite_ref-WNCCAMSBD_211-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WNCCAMSBD-211">[193]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SSOAGGRAM_212-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SSOAGGRAM-212">[194]</a></sup> The United States Congress approved the appropriations without dissent. In 2000, the budget was increased to about $68 million, in 2001 to $90 million, in 2002 to $104 million, and in 2003, to $113 million.<sup id="cite_ref-WNCCAMSBD_211-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WNCCAMSBD-211">[193]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2009, after a history of 17 years of government testing and spending of nearly $2.5 billion on research had produced almost no clearly proven efficacy of alternative therapies, Senator Harkin complained, "One of the purposes of this center was to investigate and validate alternative approaches. Quite frankly, I must say publicly that it has fallen short. It think quite frankly that in this center and in the office previously before it, most of its focus has been on disproving things rather than seeking out and approving."<sup id="cite_ref-SSOAGGRAM_212-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SSOAGGRAM-212">[194]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">[195]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-THWS_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-THWS-214">[196]</a></sup> Members of the scientific community criticized this comment as showing Senator Harkin did not understand the basics of scientific inquiry, which tests hypotheses, but never intentionally attempts to "validate approaches".<sup id="cite_ref-SSOAGGRAM_212-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SSOAGGRAM-212">[194]</a></sup> Members of the scientific and biomedical communities complained that after a history of 17 years of being tested, at a cost of over $2.5 Billion on testing scientifically and biologically implausible practices, almost no alternative therapy showed clear efficacy.<sup id="cite_ref-.242.5_billion_149-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-.242.5_billion-149">[136]</a></sup> In 2009, the NCCAM's budget was increased to about $122 million.<sup id="cite_ref-SSOAGGRAM_212-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SSOAGGRAM-212">[194]</a></sup> Overall NIH funding for CAM research increased to $300 Million by 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-SSOAGGRAM_212-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SSOAGGRAM-212">[194]</a></sup> By 2009, Americans were spending $34 Billion annually on CAM.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">[197]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2012, the <a href="/wiki/Journal_of_the_American_Medical_Association" class="mw-redirect" title="Journal of the American Medical Association">Journal of the American Medical Association</a> (JAMA) published a criticism that study after study had been funded by NCCAM, but "failed to prove that complementary or alternative therapies are anything more than placebos".<sup id="cite_ref-ITPWS_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ITPWS-216">[198]</a></sup> The JAMA criticism pointed to large wasting of research money on testing scientifically implausible treatments, citing "NCCAM officials spending $374,000 to find that inhaling lemon and lavender scents does not promote wound healing; $750,000 to find that prayer does not cure AIDS or hasten recovery from breast-reconstruction surgery; $390,000 to find that ancient Indian remedies do not control type 2 diabetes; $700,000 to find that magnets do not treat arthritis, carpal tunnel syndrome, or migraine headaches; and $406,000 to find that coffee enemas do not cure pancreatic cancer."<sup id="cite_ref-ITPWS_216-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ITPWS-216">[198]</a></sup> It was pointed out that negative results from testing were generally ignored by the public, that people continue to "believe what they want to believe, arguing that it does not matter what the data show: They know what works for them".<sup id="cite_ref-ITPWS_216-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ITPWS-216">[198]</a></sup> Continued increasing use of CAM products was also blamed on the lack of FDA ability to regulate alternative products, where negative studies do not result in FDA warnings or FDA-mandated changes on labeling, whereby few consumers are aware that many claims of many supplements were found not to have not to be supported.<sup id="cite_ref-ITPWS_216-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ITPWS-216">[198]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2014 the NCCAM was renamed to the <a href="/wiki/National_Center_for_Complementary_and_Integrative_Health" title="National Center for Complementary and Integrative Health">National Center for Complementary and Integrative Health</a> (NCCIH) with a new charter requiring that 12 of the 18 council members shall be selected with a preference to selecting leading representatives of complementary and alternative medicine, 9 of the members must be licensed practitioners of alternative medicine, 6 members must be general public leaders in the fields of public policy, law, health policy, economics, and management, and 3 members must represent the interests of individual consumers of complementary and alternative medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-NCCIHC_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIHC-217">[199]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Efficacy">Efficacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Efficacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There is a general scientific consensus that Alternative Therapies lack the requisite <a href="/wiki/Scientific_validation" class="mw-redirect" title="Scientific validation">scientific validation</a>, and their <a href="/wiki/Efficacy" title="Efficacy">effectiveness</a> is either <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">unproved</a> or <a href="/wiki/Scientific_evidence" title="Scientific evidence">disproved</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NSF_2002_10-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSF_2002-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_16-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-16">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kent1997_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kent1997-218">[200]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Goldrosen2004_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldrosen2004-219">[201]</a></sup> Many of the claims regarding the efficacy of alternative medicines are controversial, since research on them is frequently of low quality and methodologically flawed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report2005_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report2005-220">[202]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Publication_bias" title="Publication bias">Selective publication bias</a> , marked differences in product quality and standardisation, and some companies making unsubstantiated claims, call into question the claims of efficacy of isolated examples where there is evidence for alternative therapies.<sup id="cite_ref-Sarris2012_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sarris2012-221">[203]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>The Scientific Review of Alternative Medicine</i> points to confusions in the general population - a person may attribute symptomatic relief to an otherwise-ineffective therapy just because they are taking something (the placebo effect); the natural recovery from or the cyclical nature of an illness (the <a href="/wiki/Regression_fallacy" title="Regression fallacy">regression fallacy</a>) gets misattributed to an alternative medicine being taken; a person not diagnosed with science-based medicine may never originally have had a true illness diagnosed as an alternative disease category.<sup id="cite_ref-Alcock1999_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alcock1999-222">[204]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Edzard_Ernst" title="Edzard Ernst">Edzard Ernst</a> characterized the evidence for many alternative techniques as weak, nonexistent, or negative<sup id="cite_ref-goodbadugly_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-goodbadugly-223">[205]</a></sup> and in 2011 published his estimate that about 7.4% were based on "sound evidence", although he believes that may be an overestimate.<sup id="cite_ref-Ernst2011_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ernst2011-224">[206]</a></sup> Ernst has concluded that 95% of the alternative treatments he and his team studied, including acupuncture, herbal medicine, homeopathy, and reflexology, are "statistically indistinguishable from placebo treatments", but he also believes there is something that conventional doctors can usefully learn from the chiropractors and homeopath: this is the therapeutic value of the placebo effect, one of the strangest phenomena in medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-Economist2011_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Economist2011-225">[207]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HHH2008_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HHH2008-226">[208]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2003, a project funded by the CDC identified 208 condition-treatment pairs, of which 58% had been studied by at least one <a href="/wiki/Randomized_controlled_trial" title="Randomized controlled trial">randomized controlled trial</a> (RCT), and 23% had been assessed with a <a href="/wiki/Meta-analysis" title="Meta-analysis">meta-analysis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Katz2003_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Katz2003-227">[209]</a></sup> According to a 2005 book by a US <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_Medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Institute of Medicine">Institute of Medicine</a> panel, the number of RCTs focused on CAM has risen dramatically.</p>
<p>As of 2005<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the <a href="/wiki/Cochrane_Library" title="Cochrane Library">Cochrane Library</a> had 145 CAM-related Cochrane systematic reviews and 340 non-Cochrane systematic reviews. An analysis of the conclusions of only the 145 Cochrane reviews was done by two readers. In 83% of the cases, the readers agreed. In the 17% in which they disagreed, a third reader agreed with one of the initial readers to set a rating. These studies found that, for CAM, 38.4% concluded positive effect or possibly positive (12.4%), 4.8% concluded no effect, 0.69% concluded harmful effect, and 56.6% concluded insufficient evidence. An assessment of conventional treatments found that 41.3% concluded positive or possibly positive effect, 20% concluded no effect, 8.1% concluded net harmful effects, and 21.3% concluded insufficient evidence. However, the CAM review used the more developed 2004 Cochrane database, while the conventional review used the initial 1998 Cochrane database.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report2005.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fbooks.nap.edu.2Fopenbook.php.3Frecord_id.3D11182.26page.3D135_135-136.5D_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report2005.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fbooks.nap.edu.2Fopenbook.php.3Frecord_id.3D11182.26page.3D135_135-136.5D-228">[210]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the same way as for conventional therapies, drugs, and interventions, it can be difficult to test the efficacy of alternative medicine in <a href="/wiki/Clinical_trial" title="Clinical trial">clinical trials</a>. In instances where an established, effective, treatment for a condition is already available, the <a href="/wiki/Helsinki_Declaration" class="mw-redirect" title="Helsinki Declaration">Helsinki Declaration</a> states that withholding such treatment is unethical in most circumstances. Use of standard-of-care treatment in addition to an alternative technique being tested may produce confounded or difficult-to-interpret results.<sup id="cite_ref-Bodeker2002_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bodeker2002-229">[211]</a></sup></p>
<p>Cancer researcher <a href="/wiki/Andrew_J._Vickers" class="mw-redirect" title="Andrew J. Vickers">Andrew J. Vickers</a> has stated:</p>
<dl>
<dd>"Contrary to much popular and scientific writing, many <a href="/wiki/Alternative_cancer_treatments" title="Alternative cancer treatments">alternative cancer treatments</a> have been investigated in good-quality clinical trials, and they have been shown to be ineffective. The label 'unproven' is inappropriate for such therapies; it is time to assert that many alternative cancer therapies have been 'disproven'."<sup id="cite_ref-Vickers_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vickers-230">[212]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism.2C_legitimacy_and_effects">Criticism, legitimacy and effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Criticism, legitimacy and effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="rquote floatright" role="presentation" style="border-collapse:collapse; border-style:none; float:right; margin:0.5em 0.75em; width:33%;">
<tr style="text-align:left; vertical-align:top;">
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">“</td>
<td style="padding:0 10px;">There is no alternative medicine. There is only scientifically proven, evidence-based medicine supported by solid data or unproven medicine, for which scientific evidence is lacking.</td>
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; text-align:right; vertical-align:bottom; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">”</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="rquotecite" colspan="3" style="padding-top:10px; font-size:smaller; line-height:1em; text-align:right;"><cite>—&#160;P.B. Fontanarosa, <i><a href="/wiki/JAMA_(journal)" title="JAMA (journal)">Journal of the American Medical Association</a></i> (1998)<sup id="cite_ref-Fontanarosa1998_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fontanarosa1998-60">[53]</a></sup></cite></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>"CAM", meaning "complementary and alternative medicine", is not as well researched as conventional medicine, which undergoes intense research before release to the public.<sup id="cite_ref-Ernst_Cohen_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ernst_Cohen-231">[213]</a></sup> Funding for research is also sparse making it difficult to do further research for effectiveness of CAM.<sup id="cite_ref-Ernst4_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ernst4-232">[214]</a></sup> Most funding for CAM is funded by government agencies.<sup id="cite_ref-Ernst_Cohen_231-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ernst_Cohen-231">[213]</a></sup> Proposed research for CAM are rejected by most private funding agencies because the results of research are not reliable.<sup id="cite_ref-Ernst_Cohen_231-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ernst_Cohen-231">[213]</a></sup> The research for CAM has to meet certain standards from research ethics committees, which most CAM researchers find almost impossible to meet.<sup id="cite_ref-Ernst_Cohen_231-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ernst_Cohen-231">[213]</a></sup> Even with the little research done on it, CAM has not been proven to be effective.<sup id="cite_ref-Ernst3_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ernst3-233">[215]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Steven_Novella" title="Steven Novella">Steven Novella</a>, a neurologist at Yale School of Medicine, wrote that government funded studies of integrating alternative medicine techniques into the mainstream are "used to lend an appearance of legitimacy to treatments that are not legitimate."<sup id="cite_ref-Brown2009_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown2009-234">[216]</a></sup> Marcia Angell considered that critics felt that healthcare practices should be classified based solely on <a href="/wiki/Evidence-based_medicine" title="Evidence-based medicine">scientific evidence</a>, and if a treatment had been rigorously tested and found safe and effective, science-based medicine will adopt it regardless of whether it was considered "alternative" to begin with.<sup id="cite_ref-Angell1998_14-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Angell1998-14">[12]</a></sup> It is possible for a method to change categories (proven vs. unproven), based on increased knowledge of its effectiveness or lack thereof. A prominent supporter of this position is <a href="/wiki/George_D._Lundberg" title="George D. Lundberg">George D. Lundberg</a>, former editor of the <a href="/wiki/Journal_of_the_American_Medical_Association" class="mw-redirect" title="Journal of the American Medical Association">Journal of the American Medical Association</a> (JAMA).<sup id="cite_ref-Fontanarosa1998_60-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fontanarosa1998-60">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>Writing in 1999 in <i>CA: A Cancer Journal for Clinicians</i> <a href="/wiki/Barrie_R._Cassileth" title="Barrie R. Cassileth">Barrie R. Cassileth</a> mentioned a 1997 letter to the <a href="/wiki/US_Senate" class="mw-redirect" title="US Senate">US Senate</a> Subcommittee on Public Health and Safety, which had deplored the lack of critical thinking and scientific rigor in OAM-supported research, had been signed by four Nobel Laureates and other prominent scientists. (This was supported by the <a href="/wiki/National_Institutes_of_Health" title="National Institutes of Health">National Institutes of Health</a> (NIH).)<sup id="cite_ref-Cassileth1999_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cassileth1999-235">[217]</a></sup></p>
<p>In March 2009 a staff writer for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">the Washington Post</a></i> reported that the impending national discussion about broadening access to health care, improving medical practice and saving money was giving a group of scientists an opening to propose shutting down the <a href="/wiki/National_Center_for_Complementary_and_Alternative_Medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="National Center for Complementary and Alternative Medicine">National Center for Complementary and Alternative Medicine</a>. They quoted one of these scientists, <a href="/wiki/Steven_Salzberg" title="Steven Salzberg">Steven Salzberg</a>, a genome researcher and computational biologist at the University of Maryland, as saying "One of our concerns is that NIH is funding pseudoscience." They noted that the vast majority of studies were based on fundamental misunderstandings of <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Disease" title="Disease">disease</a>, and had shown little or no effect.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown2009_234-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown2009-234">[216]</a></sup></p>
<p>Writers such as <a href="/wiki/Carl_Sagan" title="Carl Sagan">Carl Sagan</a> (1934-1996), a noted astrophysicist, advocate of <a href="/wiki/Scientific_skepticism" class="mw-redirect" title="Scientific skepticism">scientific skepticism</a> and the author of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Demon-Haunted_World" title="The Demon-Haunted World">The demon–haunted world: science as a candle in the dark</a></i> (1996), have lambasted the lack of empirical evidence to support the existence of the putative energy fields on which these therapies are predicated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESagan1996_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESagan1996-131">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sampson has also pointed out that CAM tolerated <a href="/wiki/Fallacy" title="Fallacy">contradiction</a> without thorough reason and experiment.<sup id="cite_ref-SampsonAtwood2005_236-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SampsonAtwood2005-236">[218]</a></sup> Barrett has pointed out that there is a policy at the NIH of never saying something doesn't work only that a different version or dose might give different results.<sup id="cite_ref-.242.5_billion_149-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-.242.5_billion-149">[136]</a></sup> Barrett also expressed concern that, just because some "alternatives" have merit, there is the impression that the rest deserve equal consideration and respect even though most are worthless, since they are all classified under the one heading of alternative medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-Quackwatch_wary_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Quackwatch_wary-237">[219]</a></sup></p>
