<p>Some critics of alternative medicine are focused upon health fraud, misinformation, and quackery as public health problems, notably <a href="/wiki/Wallace_Sampson" title="Wallace Sampson">Wallace Sampson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paul_Kurtz" title="Paul Kurtz">Paul Kurtz</a> founders of <a href="/wiki/Scientific_Review_of_Alternative_Medicine" title="Scientific Review of Alternative Medicine">Scientific Review of Alternative Medicine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Barrett" title="Stephen Barrett">Stephen Barrett</a>, co-founder of <a href="/wiki/The_National_Council_Against_Health_Fraud" class="mw-redirect" title="The National Council Against Health Fraud">The National Council Against Health Fraud</a> and webmaster of <a href="/wiki/Quackwatch" title="Quackwatch">Quackwatch</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NCAHF_mission_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCAHF_mission-238">[220]</a></sup> Grounds for opposing alternative medicine include that:</p>
<ul>
<li>It is usually based on <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a>, tradition, <a href="/wiki/Superstition" title="Superstition">superstition</a>, belief in <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a> energies, <a href="/wiki/Pseudoscience" title="Pseudoscience">pseudoscience</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fallacy" title="Fallacy">errors in reasoning</a>, propaganda, or fraud.<sup id="cite_ref-Beyerstein2001_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beyerstein2001-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NSF_2002_10-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSF_2002-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_16-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-16">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Other_sources_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Other_sources-239">[221]</a></sup></li>
<li>Alternative therapies typically lack any <a href="/wiki/Scientific_validation" class="mw-redirect" title="Scientific validation">scientific validation</a>, and their effectiveness is either <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">unproved</a> or <a href="/wiki/Scientific_evidence" title="Scientific evidence">disproved</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_16-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-16">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kent1997_218-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kent1997-218">[200]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Goldrosen2004_219-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldrosen2004-219">[201]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (January 2013)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata">– <a href="/wiki/Talk:Alternative_medicine#Dubious" title="Talk:Alternative medicine">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup></li>
<li>Treatments are not part of the conventional, science-based healthcare system.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO_Trad_Med_Defs_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_Trad_Med_Defs-27">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NCCIH1_75-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIH1-75">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report2005.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fbooks.nap.edu.2Fopenbook.php.3Frecord_id.3D11182.26page.3D17_17-19.5D_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report2005.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fbooks.nap.edu.2Fopenbook.php.3Frecord_id.3D11182.26page.3D17_17-19.5D-240">[222]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zollman1999_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zollman1999-241">[223]</a></sup></li>
<li>Research on alternative medicine is frequently of low quality and methodologically flawed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report2005_220-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report2005-220">[202]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A more detailed citation is required. (September 2013)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li>Where alternative treatments have replaced conventional science-based medicine, even with the safest alternative medicines, failure to use or delay in using conventional science-based medicine has caused deaths.<sup id="cite_ref-Lilienfeld2002_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lilienfeld2002-242">[224]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hughes2010_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes2010-243">[225]</a></sup></li>
<li>Methods may incorporate or base themselves on <a href="/wiki/Traditional_medicine" title="Traditional medicine">traditional medicine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Home_remedy" class="mw-redirect" title="Home remedy">folk knowledge</a>, spiritual beliefs, ignorance or misunderstanding of scientific principles, errors in reasoning, or newly conceived approaches claiming to heal.<sup id="cite_ref-Beyerstein2001_8-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beyerstein2001-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_16-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-16">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Acharya2008_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Acharya2008-244">[226]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Many alternative medical treatments are not <a href="/wiki/Patent" title="Patent">patentable</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, which may lead to less research funding from the private sector. In addition, in most countries, alternative treatments (in contrast to pharmaceuticals) can be marketed without any proof of efficacy—also a disincentive for manufacturers to fund scientific research.<sup id="cite_ref-Ernst2005_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ernst2005-245">[227]</a></sup></p>
<p>English evolutionary biologist <a href="/wiki/Richard_Dawkins" title="Richard Dawkins">Richard Dawkins</a>, in his 2003 book <i><a href="/wiki/A_Devil%27s_Chaplain" title="A Devil's Chaplain">A Devil's Chaplain</a></i>, defined alternative medicine as a "set of practices that cannot be tested, refuse to be tested, or consistently fail tests."<sup id="cite_ref-Dawkins2003_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dawkins2003-246">[228]</a></sup> Dawkins argued that if a technique is demonstrated effective in properly performed trials then it ceases to be alternative and simply becomes medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-Holloway2003_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holloway2003-247">[229]</a></sup></p>
<p>CAM is also often less regulated than conventional medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-Ernst_Cohen_231-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ernst_Cohen-231">[213]</a></sup> There are ethical concerns about whether people who perform CAM have the proper knowledge to treat patients.<sup id="cite_ref-Ernst_Cohen_231-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ernst_Cohen-231">[213]</a></sup> CAM is often done by non-physicians who do not operate with the same medical licensing laws which govern conventional medicine,<sup id="cite_ref-Ernst_Cohen_231-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ernst_Cohen-231">[213]</a></sup> and it is often described as an issue of <a href="/wiki/Non-maleficence" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-maleficence">non-maleficence</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ernst2_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ernst2-248">[230]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to two writers, Wallace Sampson and K. Butler, marketing is part of the training required in alternative medicine, and propaganda methods in alternative medicine have been traced back to those used by <a href="/wiki/Hitler" class="mw-redirect" title="Hitler">Hitler</a> and <a href="/wiki/Goebels" class="mw-redirect" title="Goebels">Goebels</a> in their promotion of pseudoscience in medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_16-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-16">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ButlerBarrett1992_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ButlerBarrett1992-249">[231]</a></sup></p>
<p>In November 2011 <a href="/wiki/Edzard_Ernst" title="Edzard Ernst">Edzard Ernst</a> stated that the "level of misinformation about alternative medicine has now reached the point where it has become dangerous and unethical. So far, alternative medicine has remained an ethics-free zone. It is time to change this."<sup id="cite_ref-Ernst_Guardian_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ernst_Guardian-250">[232]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Placebo_effect">Placebo effect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Placebo effect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A research methods expert and author of "Snake Oil Science", <a href="/wiki/R._Barker_Bausell" title="R. Barker Bausell">R. Barker Bausell</a>, has stated that "it's become politically correct to investigate nonsense."<sup id="cite_ref-.242.5_billion_149-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-.242.5_billion-149">[136]</a></sup> There are concerns that just having NIH support is being used to give unfounded "legitimacy to treatments that are not legitimate."<sup id="cite_ref-Brown2009_234-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown2009-234">[216]</a></sup></p>
<p>Use of placebos to achieve a <a href="/wiki/Placebo_effect" class="mw-redirect" title="Placebo effect">placebo effect</a> in integrative medicine has been criticized as, "...diverting research time, money, and other resources from more fruitful lines of investigation in order to pursue a theory that has no basis in biology."<sup id="cite_ref-Novella2010_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Novella2010-71">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gorski2010_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gorski2010-72">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another critic has argued that academic proponents of <a href="/wiki/Integrative_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Integrative medicine">integrative medicine</a> sometimes recommend misleading patients by using known placebo treatments to achieve a placebo effect.<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">[n 19]</a></sup> However, a 2010 survey of family physicians found that 56% of respondents said they had used a placebo in clinical practice as well. Eighty-five percent of respondents believed placebos can have both psychological and physical benefits.<sup id="cite_ref-Kerman2010_253-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kerman2010-253">[234]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Integrative_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Integrative medicine">Integrative medicine</a> has been criticized in that its practitioners, trained in science-based medicine, deliberately mislead patients by pretending <a href="/wiki/Placebo" title="Placebo">placebos</a> are not. "<a href="/wiki/Quackery#Criticism_of_quackery_in_academia" title="Quackery">quackademic medicine</a>" is a pejorative term used for <i><a href="/wiki/Integrative_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Integrative medicine">integrative medicine</a></i>, which medical professionals consider an infiltration of quackery into academic <a href="/wiki/Science-based_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Science-based medicine">science-based medicine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gorski2010_72-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gorski2010-72">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>An analysis of trends in the criticism of complementary and alternative medicine (CAM) in five prestigious American medical journals during the period of reorganization within medicine (1965–1999) was reported as showing that the medical profession had responded to the growth of CAM in three phases, and that in each phase, changes in the medical marketplace had influenced the type of response in the journals.<sup id="cite_ref-Winnick2005_2009_254-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Winnick2005_2009-254">[235]</a></sup> Changes included relaxed medical licensing, the development of managed care, rising consumerism, and the establishment of the USA Office of Alternative Medicine (now <a href="/wiki/National_Center_for_Complementary_and_Alternative_Medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="National Center for Complementary and Alternative Medicine">National Center for Complementary and Alternative Medicine</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">[n 20]</a></sup> In the "condemnation" phase, from the late 1960s to the early 1970s, authors had ridiculed, exaggerated the risks, and petitioned the state to contain CAM; in the "reassessment" phase (mid-1970s through early 1990s), when increased consumer utilization of CAM was prompting concern, authors had pondered whether patient dissatisfaction and shortcomings in conventional care contributed to the trend; in the "integration" phase of the 1990s physicians began learning to work around or administer CAM, and the subjugation of CAM to scientific scrutiny had become the primary means of control.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_and_regulation">Use and regulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Use and regulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevalence_of_use">Prevalence of use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Prevalence of use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><b>Complementary and alternative medicine</b> (CAM) has been described as a broad domain of healing resources that encompasses all health systems, modalities, and practices and their accompanying theories and beliefs, other than those intrinsic to the politically dominant health system of a particular society or culture in a given historical period. CAM includes all such practices and ideas self-defined by their users as preventing or treating illness or promoting health and well-being. Boundaries within CAM and between the CAM domain and that of the dominant system are not always sharp or fixed.<sup id="cite_ref-Wieland2011_88-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wieland2011-88">[78]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (February 2013)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata">– <a href="/wiki/Talk:Alternative_medicine#Dubious" title="Talk:Alternative medicine">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup></p>
<p>According to recent research, the increasing popularity of the CAM needs to be explained by moral convictions or lifestyle choices rather than by economic reasoning.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">[237]</a></sup></p>
<p>About 50% of people in developed countries use some kind of complementary and alternative medicine other than prayer for health.<sup id="cite_ref-Barnes2004_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes2004-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ernst2003_258-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ernst2003-258">[238]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Astin1998_259-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Astin1998-259">[239]</a></sup> A British telephone survey by the BBC of 1209 adults in 1998 shows that around 20% of adults in Britain had used alternative medicine in the past 12 months.<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas2001_260-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas2001-260">[240]</a></sup> About 40% of cancer patients use some form of CAM.<sup id="cite_ref-Horneber2011_261-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horneber2011-261">[241]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">developing nations</a>, access to essential medicines is severely restricted by lack of resources and <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">poverty</a>. <a href="/wiki/Traditional_medicine" title="Traditional medicine">Traditional remedies</a>, often closely resembling or forming the basis for alternative remedies, may comprise primary healthcare or be integrated into the healthcare system. In Africa, traditional medicine is used for 80% of primary healthcare, and in developing nations as a whole over one-third of the population lack access to essential medicines.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO_tradmed_fact_262-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_tradmed_fact-262">[242]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some have proposed adopting a <a href="/wiki/Prize" title="Prize">prize</a> system to reward medical research.<sup id="cite_ref-Horrobin1986_263-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horrobin1986-263">[243]</a></sup> However, public funding for research exists. Increasing the funding for research on alternative medicine techniques is the purpose of the <a href="/wiki/US_National_Center_for_Complementary_and_Alternative_Medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="US National Center for Complementary and Alternative Medicine">US National Center for Complementary and Alternative Medicine</a>. NCCIH and its predecessor, the Office of Alternative Medicine, have spent more than $2.5 billion on such research since 1992; this research has largely not demonstrated the efficacy of alternative treatments.<sup id="cite_ref-.242.5_billion_149-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-.242.5_billion-149">[136]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nccih_.24_264-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nccih_.24-264">[244]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Atwood2003_265-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Atwood2003-265">[245]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">[246]</a></sup></p>
<p>That alternative medicine has been on the rise "in countries where Western science and scientific method generally are accepted as the major foundations for healthcare, and 'evidence-based' practice is the dominant paradigm" was described as an "<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/enigma" class="extiw" title="wikt:enigma">enigma</a>" in the Medical Journal of Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-Coulter2004_267-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coulter2004-267">[247]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_US">In the US</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: In the US">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the United States, the 1974 <a href="/wiki/Child_Abuse_Prevention_and_Treatment_Act" title="Child Abuse Prevention and Treatment Act">Child Abuse Prevention and Treatment Act</a> (CAPTA) required that for states to receive federal money, they had to grant religious exemptions to <a href="/wiki/Child_neglect" title="Child neglect">child neglect</a> and <a href="/wiki/Child_abuse" title="Child abuse">abuse</a> laws regarding religion-based healing practices.<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">[248]</a></sup> Thirty-one states have child-abuse religious exemptions.<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">[249]</a></sup></p>
<p>The use of alternative medicine in the US has increased,<sup id="cite_ref-NSF_2002_10-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSF_2002-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Eisenberg1998_187-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eisenberg1998-187">[170]</a></sup> with a 50 percent increase in expenditures and a 25 percent increase in the use of alternative therapies between 1990 and 1997 in America.<sup id="cite_ref-Eisenberg1998_187-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eisenberg1998-187">[170]</a></sup> Americans spend many billions on the therapies annually.<sup id="cite_ref-Eisenberg1998_187-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eisenberg1998-187">[170]</a></sup> Most Americans used CAM to treat and/or prevent musculoskeletal conditions or other conditions associated with chronic or recurring pain.<sup id="cite_ref-Barnes2004_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes2004-9">[9]</a></sup> In America, women were more likely than men to use CAM, with the biggest difference in use of <a href="/wiki/Terms_and_concepts_in_alternative_medicine#Mind-body_interventions" class="mw-redirect" title="Terms and concepts in alternative medicine">mind-body therapies</a> including prayer specifically for health reasons".<sup id="cite_ref-Barnes2004_9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes2004-9">[9]</a></sup> In 2008, more than 37% of American hospitals offered alternative therapies, up from 26.5 percent in 2005, and 25% in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-Warner2006_270-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Warner2006-270">[250]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AHA2008_271-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AHA2008-271">[251]</a></sup> More than 70% of the hospitals offering CAM were in urban areas.<sup id="cite_ref-AHA2008_271-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AHA2008-271">[251]</a></sup></p>
<p>A survey of Americans found that 88 percent thought that "there are some good ways of treating sickness that medical science does not recognize".<sup id="cite_ref-NSF_2002_10-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSF_2002-10">[10]</a></sup> Use of magnets was the most common tool in <a href="/wiki/Energy_medicine" title="Energy medicine">energy medicine</a> in America, and among users of it, 58 percent described it as at least "sort of <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific</a>", when it is not at all scientific.<sup id="cite_ref-NSF_2002_10-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSF_2002-10">[10]</a></sup> In 2002, at least 60 percent of US medical schools have at least some class time spent teaching alternative therapies.<sup id="cite_ref-NSF_2002_10-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSF_2002-10">[10]</a></sup> "Therapeutic touch", was taught at more than 100 colleges and universities in 75 countries before the practice was debunked by a nine-year-old child for a school science project.<sup id="cite_ref-NSF_2002_10-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSF_2002-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rosa1998_272-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rosa1998-272">[252]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevalence_of_use_of_specific_therapies">Prevalence of use of specific therapies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Prevalence of use of specific therapies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The most common CAM therapies used in the US in 2002 were prayer (45.2%), <a href="/wiki/Herbalism" title="Herbalism">herbalism</a> (18.9%), <a href="/wiki/Terms_and_concepts_in_alternative_medicine#Breathing_Meditation" class="mw-redirect" title="Terms and concepts in alternative medicine">breathing meditation</a> (11.6%), <a href="/wiki/Meditation_(alternative_medicine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Meditation (alternative medicine)">meditation</a> (7.6%), <a href="/wiki/Chiropractic_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Chiropractic medicine">chiropractic medicine</a> (7.5%), <a href="/wiki/Yoga_(alternative_medicine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Yoga (alternative medicine)">yoga</a> (5.1%-6.1%), <a href="/wiki/Body_work_(alternative_medicine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Body work (alternative medicine)">body work</a> (5.0%), <a href="/wiki/Terms_and_concepts_in_alternative_medicine#Diet-based_therapy" class="mw-redirect" title="Terms and concepts in alternative medicine">diet-based therapy</a> (3.5%), <a href="/wiki/Terms_and_concepts_in_alternative_medicine#Progressive_Relaxation" class="mw-redirect" title="Terms and concepts in alternative medicine">progressive relaxation</a> (3.0%), <a href="/wiki/Orthomolecular_medicine" title="Orthomolecular medicine">mega-vitamin therapy</a> (2.8%) and <a href="/wiki/Visualization_(cam)" class="mw-redirect" title="Visualization (cam)">Visualization</a> (2.1%)<sup id="cite_ref-Barnes2004_9-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes2004-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Barnes2008_273-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes2008-273">[253]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Britain, the most often used alternative therapies were <a href="/wiki/Alexander_technique" title="Alexander technique">Alexander technique</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aromatherapy" title="Aromatherapy">Aromatherapy</a>, Bach and other flower remedies, Body work therapies including massage, Counseling stress therapies, <a href="/wiki/Hypnotherapy" title="Hypnotherapy">hypnotherapy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meditation" title="Meditation">Meditation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reflexology" title="Reflexology">Reflexology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shiatsu" title="Shiatsu">Shiatsu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ayurvedic_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Ayurvedic medicine">Ayurvedic medicine</a>, Nutritional medicine, and <a href="/wiki/Yoga" title="Yoga">Yoga</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESir_Walton:_Science_and_Technology_Committee2000_274-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESir_Walton:_Science_and_Technology_Committee2000-274">[254]</a></sup> Ayurvedic medicine remedies are mainly plant based with some use of animal materials.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWujastyk2003_275-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWujastyk2003-275">[255]</a></sup> Safety concerns include the use of herbs containing toxic compounds and the lack of quality control in Ayurvedic facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-Valiathan2006_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Valiathan2006-128">[115]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Saper2008_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Saper2008-130">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Service_(England)" title="National Health Service (England)">National Health Service (England)</a>, the most commonly used complementary and alternative medicines (CAM) supported by the NHS in the UK are: acupuncture, aromatherapy, chiropractic, homeopathy, massage, osteopathy and clinical hypnotherapy.<sup id="cite_ref-NHS_Careers_CAM_276-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NHS_Careers_CAM-276">[256]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="In_palliative_care">In palliative care</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: In palliative care">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Complementary therapies are often used in <a href="/wiki/Palliative_care" title="Palliative care">palliative care</a> or by practitioners attempting to manage chronic pain in patients. Integrative medicine is considered more acceptable in the interdisciplinary approach used in palliative care than in other areas of medicine. "From its early experiences of care for the dying, palliative care took for granted the necessity of placing patient values and lifestyle habits at the core of any design and delivery of quality care at the end of life. If the patient desired complementary therapies, and as long as such treatments provided additional support and did not endanger the patient, they were considered acceptable."<sup id="cite_ref-Kellhear2003_277-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kellhear2003-277">[257]</a></sup> The non-pharmacologic interventions of complementary medicine can employ <a href="/wiki/Mind-body_intervention" class="mw-redirect" title="Mind-body intervention">mind-body</a> interventions designed to "reduce pain and concomitant mood disturbance and increase quality of life."<sup id="cite_ref-Menefee2005_278-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Menefee2005-278">[258]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulation">Regulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Regulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Regulation_of_alternative_medicine" title="Regulation of alternative medicine">Regulation of alternative medicine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Regulation_and_prevalence_of_homeopathy" title="Regulation and prevalence of homeopathy">Regulation and prevalence of homeopathy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Use_Caution_With_Ayurvedic_Products_(FDA_October_16,_2008).djvu" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Use_Caution_With_Ayurvedic_Products_%28FDA_October_16%2C_2008%29.djvu/page1-220px-Use_Caution_With_Ayurvedic_Products_%28FDA_October_16%2C_2008%29.djvu.jpg" width="220" height="285" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Use_Caution_With_Ayurvedic_Products_%28FDA_October_16%2C_2008%29.djvu/page1-330px-Use_Caution_With_Ayurvedic_Products_%28FDA_October_16%2C_2008%29.djvu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Use_Caution_With_Ayurvedic_Products_%28FDA_October_16%2C_2008%29.djvu/page1-440px-Use_Caution_With_Ayurvedic_Products_%28FDA_October_16%2C_2008%29.djvu.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2550" data-file-height="3301" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Use_Caution_With_Ayurvedic_Products_(FDA_October_16,_2008).djvu" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Health campaign flyers, as in this example from the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">Food and Drug Administration</a>, warn the public about unsafe products.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In Austria and Germany complementary and alternative medicine is mainly in the hands of doctors with <a href="/wiki/Medical_degree" title="Medical degree">MDs</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-ernstinterview_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ernstinterview-55">[48]</a></sup> and half or more of the American alternative practitioners are licensed MDs.<sup id="cite_ref-Cassileth1996_279-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cassileth1996-279">[259]</a></sup> In Germany herbs are tightly regulated: half are prescribed by doctors and covered by health insurance.<sup id="cite_ref-JAMA_book_review_280-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JAMA_book_review-280">[260]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some professions of complementary/traditional/alternative medicine, such as <a href="/wiki/Chiropractic" title="Chiropractic">chiropractic</a>, have achieved full regulation in North America and other parts of the world<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWHO2005_281-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWHO2005-281">[261]</a></sup> and are regulated in a manner similar to that governing science-based medicine. In contrast, other approaches may be partially recognized and others have no regulation at all. Regulation and licensing of alternative medicine ranges widely from country to country, and state to state.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWHO2005_281-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWHO2005-281">[261]</a></sup></p>
<p>Government bodies in the USA and elsewhere have published information or guidance about alternative medicine. The <a href="/wiki/U.S._Food_and_Drug_Administration" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Food and Drug Administration">U.S. Food and Drug Administration</a> (FDA), has issued online warnings for consumers about medication health fraud.<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">[262]</a></sup> This includes a section on Alternative Medicine Fraud,<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">[263]</a></sup> such as a warning that Ayurvedic products generally have not been approved by the FDA before marketing.<sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">[264]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conflicts_of_interest">Conflicts of interest</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Conflicts of interest">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Some commentators have said that special consideration must be given to the issue of <a href="/wiki/Conflict_of_interest" title="Conflict of interest">conflicts of interest</a> in alternative medicine. <a href="/wiki/Edzard_Ernst" title="Edzard Ernst">Edzard Ernst</a> has said that most researchers into alternative medicine are at risk of "unidirectional bias" because of a generally uncritical belief in their chosen subject.<sup id="cite_ref-unid_285-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unid-285">[265]</a></sup> Ernst cites as evidence the phenomenon whereby 100% of a sample of acupuncture trials originating in China had positive conclusions.<sup id="cite_ref-unid_285-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unid-285">[265]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/David_Gorski" title="David Gorski">David Gorski</a> contrasts <a href="/wiki/Evidence-based_medicine" title="Evidence-based medicine">evidence-based medicine</a>, in which researchers try to disprove hyphotheses, with what he says is the frequent practice in pseudoscience-based research, of striving to confirm pre-existing notions.<sup id="cite_ref-gorski_286-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gorski-286">[266]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Harriet_A._Hall" title="Harriet A. Hall">Harriet A. Hall</a> writes that there is a contrast between the circumstances of alternative medicine practitioners and disinterested scientists: in the case of acupuncture, for example, an acupuncturist would have "a great deal to lose" if acupuncture were rejected by research; but the disinterested skeptic would not lose anything if its effects were confirmed; rather their change of mind would enhance their skeptical credentials.<sup id="cite_ref-hall_287-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hall-287">[267]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Risks">Risks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Risks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_herbs_with_known_adverse_effects" title="List of herbs with known adverse effects">List of herbs with known adverse effects</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adequacy_of_regulation_and_CAM_safety">Adequacy of regulation and CAM safety</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Adequacy of regulation and CAM safety">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many of the claims regarding the safety and efficacy of alternative medicine are controversial. Some alternative treatments have been associated with unexpected side effects, which can be fatal.<sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288">[268]</a></sup></p>
<p>A commonly voiced concerns about complementary alternative medicine (CAM) is the way it's regulated. There have been significant developments in how CAMs should be assessed prior to re-sale in the United Kingdom and the European Union (EU) in the last 2 years. Despite this, it has been suggested that current regulatory bodies have been ineffective in preventing deception of patients as many companies have re-labelled their drugs to avoid the new laws.<sup id="cite_ref-Colquhoun2012_289-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Colquhoun2012-289">[269]</a></sup> There is no general consensus about how to balance consumer protection (from false claims, toxicity, and advertising) with freedom to choose remedies.</p>
<p>Advocates of CAM suggest that regulation of the industry will adversely affect patients looking for alternative ways to manage their symptoms, even if many of the benefits may represent the placebo affect.<sup id="cite_ref-Connelly2012_290-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Connelly2012-290">[270]</a></sup> Some contend that alternative medicines should not require any more regulation than over-the-counter medicines that can also be toxic in overdose (such as paracetamol).<sup id="cite_ref-Malcolm2012_291-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Malcolm2012-291">[271]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interactions_with_conventional_pharmaceuticals">Interactions with conventional pharmaceuticals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Interactions with conventional pharmaceuticals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Forms of alternative medicine that are biologically active can be dangerous even when used in conjunction with conventional medicine. Examples include immuno-augmentation therapy, shark cartilage, bioresonance therapy, oxygen and ozone therapies, and insulin potentiation therapy. Some herbal remedies can cause dangerous interactions with chemotherapy drugs, radiation therapy, or anesthetics during surgery, among other problems.<sup id="cite_ref-CassilethDeng2004_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CassilethDeng2004-56">[49]</a></sup> An anecdotal example of these dangers was reported by Associate Professor Alastair MacLennan of Adelaide University, Australia regarding a patient who almost bled to death on the operating table after neglecting to mention that she had been taking "natural" potions to "build up her strength" before the operation, including a powerful anticoagulant that nearly caused her death.<sup id="cite_ref-Hills1999_292-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hills1999-292">[272]</a></sup></p>
<p>To <i>ABC Online</i>, MacLennan also gives another possible mechanism:</p>
<dl>
<dd>And lastly  [<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>] there's the cynicism and disappointment and depression that some patients get from going on from one alternative medicine to the next, and they find after three months the placebo effect wears off, and they're disappointed and they move on to the next one, and they're disappointed and disillusioned, and that can create depression and make the eventual treatment of the patient with anything effective difficult, because you may not get compliance, because they've seen the failure so often in the past.<sup id="cite_ref-SwanABC_293-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SwanABC-293">[273]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Potential_side-effects">Potential side-effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Potential side-effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Conventional treatments are subjected to testing for undesired <a href="/wiki/Adverse_effect_(medicine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Adverse effect (medicine)">side-effects</a>, whereas alternative treatments, in general, are not subjected to such testing at all. Any treatment – whether conventional or alternative – that has a biological or psychological effect on a patient may also have potential to possess dangerous biological or psychological side-effects. Attempts to refute this fact with regard to alternative treatments sometimes use the <i><a href="/wiki/Appeal_to_nature" title="Appeal to nature">appeal to nature</a></i> fallacy, i.e., "That which is natural cannot be harmful." Specific groups of patients such as patients with impaired hepatic or renal function are more susceptible to side effects of alternative remedies.<sup id="cite_ref-294" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-294">[274]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295">[275]</a></sup></p>
<p>An exception to the normal thinking regarding side-effects is <a href="/wiki/Homeopathy" title="Homeopathy">Homeopathy</a>. Since 1938, the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Food_and_Drug_Administration" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Food and Drug Administration">U.S. Food and Drug Administration</a> (FDA) has regulated homeopathic products in "several significantly different ways from other drugs."<sup id="cite_ref-Stehlin_296-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stehlin-296">[276]</a></sup> Homeopathic preparations, termed "remedies", are extremely dilute, often far beyond the point where a single molecule of the original active (and possibly toxic) ingredient is likely to remain. They are, thus, considered safe on that count, but "their products are exempt from good manufacturing practice requirements related to expiration dating and from finished product testing for identity and strength", and their alcohol concentration may be much higher than allowed in conventional drugs.<sup id="cite_ref-Stehlin_296-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stehlin-296">[276]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Treatment_delay">Treatment delay</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Treatment delay">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Those having experienced or perceived success with one alternative therapy for a minor ailment may be convinced of its efficacy and persuaded to extrapolate that success to some other alternative therapy for a more serious, possibly life-threatening illness.<sup id="cite_ref-Navarro2006_297-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Navarro2006-297">[277]</a></sup> For this reason, critics argue that therapies that rely on the placebo effect to define success are very dangerous. According to mental health journalist Scott Lilienfeld in 2002, "unvalidated or scientifically unsupported mental health practices can lead individuals to forgo effective treatments" and refers to this as "<a href="/wiki/Opportunity_cost" title="Opportunity cost">opportunity cost</a>". Individuals who spend large amounts of time and money on ineffective treatments may be left with precious little of either, and may forfeit the opportunity to obtain treatments that could be more helpful. In short, even innocuous treatments can indirectly produce negative outcomes.<sup id="cite_ref-Lilienfeld2002_242-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lilienfeld2002-242">[224]</a></sup> Between 2001 and 2003, four children died in Australia because their parents chose ineffective naturopathic, homeopathic, or other alternative medicines and diets rather than conventional therapies.<sup id="cite_ref-Hughes2010_243-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes2010-243">[225]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unconventional_cancer_.22cures.22">Unconventional cancer "cures"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Unconventional cancer &quot;cures&quot;">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There have always been "many therapies offered outside of conventional cancer treatment centers and based on theories not found in <a href="/wiki/Biomedicine" title="Biomedicine">biomedicine</a>. These alternative cancer cures have often been described as 'unproven,' suggesting that appropriate clinical trials have not been conducted and that the therapeutic value of the treatment is unknown." However, "many alternative cancer treatments have been investigated in good-quality clinical trials, and they have been shown to be ineffective....The label 'unproven' is inappropriate for such therapies; it is time to assert that many alternative cancer therapies have been 'disproven'."<sup id="cite_ref-Vickers_230-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vickers-230">[212]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Edzard_Ernst" title="Edzard Ernst">Edzard Ernst</a> has stated:</p>
<dl>
<dd>"... any alternative cancer cure is bogus by definition. There will never be an alternative cancer cure. Why? Because if something looked halfway promising, then mainstream oncology would scrutinize it, and if there is anything to it, it would become mainstream almost automatically and very quickly. All curative "alternative cancer cures" are based on false claims, are bogus, and, I would say, even criminal."<sup id="cite_ref-Miller_298-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miller-298">[278]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-count references-column-count-2" style="-moz-column-count: 2; -webkit-column-count: 2; column-count: 2; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-NSFquote-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-NSFquote_11-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-NSFquote_11-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">"[A]lternative medicine refers to all treatments that have not been proven effective using scientific methods."<sup id="cite_ref-NSF_2002_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSF_2002-10">[10]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-IOMquote-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-IOMquote_13-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-IOMquote_13-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-IOMquote_13-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-IOMquote_13-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">"Complementary and alternative medicine (CAM) is a broad domain of resources that encompasses health systems, modalities, and practices and their accompanying theories and beliefs, other than those intrinsic to the dominant health system of a particular society or culture in a given historical period. CAM includes such resources perceived by their users as associated with positive health outcomes. Boundaries within CAM and between the CAM domain and the domain of the dominant system are not always sharp or fixed."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report200519_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report200519-12">[11]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Angellquote-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Angellquote_15-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Angellquote_15-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">"It is time for the scientific community to stop giving alternative medicine a free ride. There cannot be two kinds of medicine – conventional and alternative. There is only medicine that has been adequately tested and medicine that has not, medicine that works and medicine that may or may not work... speculation, and testimonials do not substitute for evidence."<sup id="cite_ref-Angell1998_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Angell1998-14">[12]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Harrisonquote-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Harrisonquote_21-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Harrisonquote_21-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Harrisonquote_21-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">"The phrase complementary and alternative medicine is used to describe a group of diverse medical and health care systems, practices, and products that have historic origins outside mainstream medicine. Most of these practices are used together with conventional therapies and therefore have been called complementary to distinguish them from alternative practices, those used as a substitute for standard care. ... Until a decade ago or so, "complementary and alternative medicine" could be defined as practices that are neither taught in medical schools nor reimbursed, but this definition is no longer workable, since medical students increasingly seek and receive some instruction about complementary health practices, and some practices are reimbursed by third-party payers. Another definition, practices that lack an evidence base, is also not useful, since there is a growing body of research on some of these modalities, and some aspects of standard care do not have a strong evidence base."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarrison.27s_Principles_of_Internal_Medicine20151chpt._14-E_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarrison.27s_Principles_of_Internal_Medicine20151chpt._14-E-20">[17]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-WebMD2014quote-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-WebMD2014quote_23-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"An alternative medical system is a set of practices based on a philosophy different from Western biomedicine."<sup id="cite_ref-WebMD2014_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WebMD2014-22">[18]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-NCCIH2quote-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-NCCIH2quote_25-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"CAM is a group of diverse medical and health care systems, practices, and products that are not generally considered part of conventional medicine."<sup id="cite_ref-NCCIH2_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIH2-24">[19]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-FinalReport-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FinalReport_37-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <i>Final Report</i> (2002) of the White House Commission on Complementary and Alternative Medicine Policy states: "The Commissioners believe and have repeatedly stated in this Report that our response should be to hold all systems of health and healing, including conventional and CAM, to the same rigorous standards of good science and health services research. Although the Commissioners support the provision of the most accurate information about the state of the science of all CAM modalities, they believe that it is premature to advocate the wide implementation and reimbursement of CAM modalities that are yet unproven."<sup id="cite_ref-whccamp.hhs.gov_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whccamp.hhs.gov-36">[30]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-64"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-64">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Mary Ruggie in Chapter 2 of <i>Marginal to Mainstream: Alternative Medicine in America</i> said, "By the mid-1990s, the notion that some alternative therapies could be complementary to conventional medicine began to change the status of...alternative medicine. The 21st century is witnessing yet another terminological innovation, in which CAM and conventional medicine are becoming integrative."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERuggie2004_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERuggie2004-63">[56]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-78"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-78">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As David J. Hufford, Professor and Director at the Doctors Kienle Center for Humanistic Medicine at the <a href="/wiki/Penn_State_Milton_S._Hershey_Medical_Center" title="Penn State Milton S. Hershey Medical Center">Penn State College of Medicine</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-HuffordUPenn_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HuffordUPenn-76">[68]</a></sup> has argued: "Simply because an herbal remedy comes to be used by physicians does not mean that herbalists cease to practice, or that the practice of the one becomes like that of the other."<sup id="cite_ref-Hufford2004a_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hufford2004a-77">[69]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-81"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-81">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The BMA used the term non-conventional medicine instead of alternative medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-BMA_1993_1st_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BMA_1993_1st-80">[71]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-90"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-90">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Office for Alternative Medicine, part of the National Institutes of Health, was renamed NCCAM in 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-FDA_regulatory_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FDA_regulatory-89">[79]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-103"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-103">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Evidence based medicine is the conscientious, explicit, and judicious use of current best evidence in making decisions about the care of individual patients";<sup id="cite_ref-Sackett1996_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sackett1996-102">[91]</a></sup> "Evidence based medicine, whose philosophical origins extend back to mid-19th century Paris and earlier, remains a hot topic for clinicians, public health practitioners, purchasers, planners, and the public. British centres for evidence based practice have been established or planned in adult medicine, child health, surgery, pathology, pharmacotherapy, nursing, general practice, and dentistry; the Cochrane Collaboration and Britain's Centre for Review and Dissemination in York are providing systematic reviews of the effects of health care".<sup id="cite_ref-Sackett1996_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sackett1996-102">[91]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Hahnemannquote-116"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Hahnemannquote_116-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In his book <i>The Homœopathic Medical Doctrine</i> <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Hahnemann" title="Samuel Hahnemann">Samuel Hahnemann</a> the creator of homeopathy wrote: "Observation, reflection, and experience have unfolded to me that the best and true method of cure is founded on the principle, <i><a href="/wiki/Similia_similibus_curentur" class="mw-redirect" title="Similia similibus curentur">similia similibus curentur</a>.</i> To cure in a mild, prompt, safe, and durable manner, it is necessary to choose in each case a medicine that will excite an affection similar (<i><span lang="el" xml:lang="el">ὅμοιος πάθος</span></i>) to that against which it is employed."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHahnemann1833.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DEnEFAAAAQAAJ.26pg.3DPR3_iii.5D.2C_.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DEnEFAAAAQAAJ.26pg.3DPA48_48.E2.80.9349.5D_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHahnemann1833.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DEnEFAAAAQAAJ.26pg.3DPR3_iii.5D.2C_.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DEnEFAAAAQAAJ.26pg.3DPA48_48.E2.80.9349.5D-115">[103]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-166"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-166">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Peking_University_Health_Science_Center" title="Peking University Health Science Center">Peking University Health Science Center</a> (formerly Beijing Medical University) was the first of the kind in China to teach western medicine and train medical professionals.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-167"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-167">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For an encyclopaedic account of the development of "western" medicine in the period leading up to the reforms in the medical schools of US resulting from the Flexner Report, published at the time of that report, see the article, <img alt="Wikisource-logo.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/12px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png" width="12" height="13" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/18px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/24px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="410" data-file-height="430" />&#160;<cite class="citation encyclopaedia"><a href="/wiki/Clifford_Allbutt" title="Clifford Allbutt">Allbutt, Thomas Clifford</a> (1911). "<a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/1911_Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica/Medicine" class="extiw" title="wikisource:1911 Encyclopædia Britannica/Medicine">Medicine</a>". In Chisholm, Hugh. <i><a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica_Eleventh_Edition" title="Encyclopædia Britannica Eleventh Edition">Encyclopædia Britannica</a></i>. <b>18</b> (11th ed.). Cambridge University Press.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAlternative+medicine&amp;rft.atitle=Medicine&amp;rft.aufirst=Thomas+Clifford&amp;rft.aulast=Allbutt&amp;rft.btitle=Encyclop%C3%A6dia+Britannica&amp;rft.date=1911&amp;rft.edition=11th&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-175"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-175">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In his introduction to the Flexner Report, Henry S. Pritchett stated, "The fundamental sciences upon which medicine depends have been greatly extended. The laboratory has come to furnish alike to the physician and to the surgeon a new means for diagnosing and combating disease. The education of the medical practitioner under these changed conditions makes entirely different demands in respect of both preliminary and professional training."<sup id="cite_ref-Pritchett1910_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pritchett1910-174">[159]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-181"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-181">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The earliest occurrence of the term "alternative medicine" in an English language publication was only in 1974, according to the <a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-OED_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OED-180">[164]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-191"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-191">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As the medical professor <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Ludmerer" title="Kenneth Ludmerer">Kenneth M. Ludmerer</a> noted in 2010: "Flexner pointed out that the scientific method of thinking applied to medical practice. By scientific method, he meant testing ideas with well-planned experiments to establish accurate facts. The clinician's diagnosis was equivalent to the scientist's hypothesis: both <a href="/wiki/Medical_diagnosis" title="Medical diagnosis">medical diagnosis</a> and hypothesis required the test of an experiment. Flexner argued that mastery of the scientific method of problem solving was the key for physicians to manage medical uncertainty and to practice in the most cost-effective way."<sup id="cite_ref-Ludmerer2010_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ludmerer2010-190">[173]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-252"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-252">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As a 2010 article in the <i>New England Journal of Medicine</i> concluded:</span>
<blockquote>
<p><span class="reference-text">real acupuncture treatments were no more effective than sham acupuncture treatments. There was, nevertheless, evidence that both real acupuncture and sham acupuncture were more effective than no treatment, and that acupuncture can be a useful supplement to other forms of conventional therapy for low back pain.<sup id="cite_ref-Berman2010_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berman2010-251">[233]</a></sup></span></p>
</blockquote>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-256"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-256">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to the medical historian <a href="/wiki/James_Harvey_Young" title="James Harvey Young">James Harvey Young</a>:</span>
<blockquote>
<p><span class="reference-text">In 1991 the Senate Appropriations Committee responsible for funding the National Institutes of Health (NIH) declared itself "not satisfied that the conventional medical community as symbolized at the NIH has fully explored the potential that exists in unconventional medical practices.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">[236]</a></sup></span></p>
</blockquote>
</li>
</ol>
</div>

<li id="cite_note-132"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-132">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Rosa L, Rosa E, Sarner L, Barrett S (April 1998). "A close look at therapeutic touch". <i>JAMA</i>. <b>279</b>: 1005–10. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1001%2Fjama.279.13.1005">10.1001/jama.279.13.1005</a>. <a href="/wiki/PubMed_Identifier" class="mw-redirect" title="PubMed Identifier">PMID</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/9533499">9533499</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAlternative+medicine&amp;rft.atitle=A+close+look+at+therapeutic+touch&amp;rft.au=Rosa+L%2C+Rosa+E%2C+Sarner+L%2C+Barrett+S&amp;rft.date=1998-04&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1001%2Fjama.279.13.1005&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F9533499&amp;rft.jtitle=JAMA&amp;rft.pages=1005-10&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=279" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span> <span class="citation-comment" style="display:none; color:#33aa33">CS1 maint: Multiple names: authors list (<a href="/wiki/Category:CS1_maint:_Multiple_names:_authors_list" title="Category:CS1 maint: Multiple names: authors list">link</a>)</span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Abdulla1999-133"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Abdulla1999_133-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Abdulla1999_133-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Abdulla1999_133-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Abdulla1999_133-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Abdulla, Sara (May 13, 1999). "Phytotherapy - good science or big business?". <i>Nature</i>. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1038%2Fnews990513-8">10.1038/news990513-8</a>. <q>Science-based medicine, with its emphasis on controlled study, proof, evidence, statistical significance and safety is being rejected in favour of 'alternative medicine'—an atavistic portmanteau of anecdote, hearsay, rumour and hokum.... Probably the most commercially successful and widely used branch of alternative or complementary medicine is 'phytotherapy'. These are the tablets, powders and elixirs, otherwise known as herbal medicine, that are sold in most countries, through health shops and pharmacies as 'nutritional supplements'.... Only a tiny minority of these remedies have been shown to have mild-to moderately beneficial health effects... So why are affluent, otherwise rational, highly educated people (for this is the average user profile) so hungry for phytotherapy?... people still believe that 'natural' equals good and safe despite plenty of evidence to the contrary." ... as far as the human body is concerned, 'natural' is meaningless... Equally, what's so safe about consuming substances that need meet no standards of contents? ...</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAlternative+medicine&amp;rft.atitle=Phytotherapy+-+good+science+or+big+business%3F&amp;rft.aufirst=Sara&amp;rft.aulast=Abdulla&amp;rft.date=1999-05-13&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1038%2Fnews990513-8&amp;rft.jtitle=Nature&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_Health_Organization">World Health Organization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: World Health Organization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.theness.com/index.php/what-is-complementary-and-alternative-medicine/">What is Complementary and Alternative Medicine?</a> – <a href="/wiki/Steven_Novella" title="Steven Novella">Steven Novella</a>, Maryland</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.skepdic.com/althelth.html">"Alternative" health practice</a> – <a href="/wiki/Skeptic%27s_Dictionary" class="mw-redirect" title="Skeptic's Dictionary">Skeptic's Dictionary</a></li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.quackwatch.org">Quackwatch.org</a> – <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Barrett" title="Stephen Barrett">Stephen Barrett</a> (See also: <a href="/wiki/Quackwatch" title="Quackwatch">Quackwatch</a>)</li>
<li><cite id="CITEREFPurday2009" class="citation journal">Purday, K.M. (2009-01-27). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://metapsychology.mentalhelp.net/poc/view_doc.php?type=book&amp;id=4690&amp;cn=452">"Review - <i>Healing, Hype, or Harm? A Critical Analysis of Complementary or Alternative Medicine</i>, by Edzard Ernst (Editor)"</a>. <i>Metapsychology online reviews</i>. <b>13</b> (5).</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAlternative+medicine&amp;rft.atitle=Review+-+Healing%2C+Hype%2C+or+Harm%3F+A+Critical+Analysis+of+Complementary+or+Alternative+Medicine%2C+by+Edzard+Ernst+%28Editor%29&amp;rft.aufirst=K.M.&amp;rft.aulast=Purday&amp;rft.date=2009-01-27&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fmetapsychology.mentalhelp.net%2Fpoc%2Fview_doc.php%3Ftype%3Dbook%26id%3D4690%26cn%3D452&amp;rft.issue=5&amp;rft.jtitle=Metapsychology+online+reviews&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=13" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://whatstheharm.net/">What's the harm?</a> Website created by Tim Farley listing cases of people harmed by various alternative treatments</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=RWbkvCMuU5A/">The Alternative Medicine Racket</a> A video investigation of state-supported quackery at the National Institutes of Health. – <a href="/wiki/Reason_TV" class="mw-redirect" title="Reason TV">Reason TV</a></li>
</ul>
